The Third Proof: Coexistence of Similars Is Contingent and Contradictory
Al-Jurjani presents the third proof against coexistence of similars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's third proof that the coexistence of two similars in one locus is impossible because their coexistence would be contingent and lead to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
If similars could coexist, one could vanish while the other remains—a contradiction.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a third line of argument against the possibility of two similars coexisting in one locus. He shows that even if we grant such coexistence, it would not be necessary; one similar could cease while the other remains. This leads to an absurd consequence that undermines the very notion of similarity. Let's work through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Notice the parentheses indicating the main text and the commentary.
The passage is dense. Focus on the logical flow first.
2
Understand the assumption and its consequence
The proof assumes for argument's sake that two similars (e.g., two blacknesses) can coexist. It then argues that if they do, their coexistence is not necessary; one could cease while the other remains.
This is the key move: 'it is not necessary such that its removal after its occurrence is impossible.'
3
Trace the resulting contradiction
If one similar is removed, the locus can acquire the opposite of that removed similar. This would mean the locus simultaneously has one similar and the opposite of the other—an impossibility.
The passage mentions motion and endpoints to illustrate the removal of opposites.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that the original assumption (coexistence of two similars) leads to a contradiction, so it must be false.
This reinforces the Ash'ari position that two similars are like opposites and cannot coexist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه أي الاجتماع على تقدير جوازه ( لا يجب ) بحيث يمتنع زواله بعد حصوله فإذا اجتمع سوادان مثلا في محل واحد، جاز أن ينتفي عنه أحدهما بقاء الآخر، وإذا انتفى عن المحل أحد المثلين ( فيجوز مع اتصافه ) أي اتصاف ذلك المحل ) بضد (المثل ( المنتفي ؛ لأن زوال أحد الضدين عن تماثلهما باتحاد المتحرك وما فيه الحركة والمبدأ والمنتهى وإذا كان كذلك ترتفع الاثنينية عنهما .
The third: That is, that coming together, on the assumption of its permissibility, (is not necessary) such that its removal after its occurrence is impossible. So if two blacknesses, for example, come together in one locus, it is possible that one of them is removed while the other remains. And if one of the two similars is removed from the locus, (then it is permissible along with its characterization), i.e., the characterization of that locus, with the opposite (of the removed similar); because the removal of one of the two opposites due to their similarity is by the union of the moving object and what contains the motion and the starting point and endpoint. And when that is so, twoness is removed from them.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. The argument is that if two similars could coexist, their coexistence would not be necessary, meaning that it would be possible for one to cease while the other remains. For example, if two blacknesses coexist in one substance, it would be permissible for one to vanish while the other persists. Then, after one of the similars is removed, the substance can acquire the opposite of that removed similar. This leads to an absurdity because the substance would simultaneously have one blackness and a non-blackness (or its opposite) while still sharing in the similarity with the removed one? The text is somewhat compressed. The key idea is that if similars can coexist, then they are not necessarily linked, so the removal of one while the other remains is possible. But then the remaining similar would be in the same locus with the opposite of the removed one, which contradicts the notion that similars are opposites. The proof relies on the Ash'ari position that two similars are like opposites. Therefore, the coexistence of two similars would entail that they are not true opposites, and thus the impossibility is shown. The commentary adds that the removal of one opposite due to their similarity is by the union of the moving object, etc., which may refer to the conditions for motion and change.

Key Terms
لا يجب
زوال
بقاء
ضد
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two similars can coexist, they must be able to persist together necessarily.
Correction: The proof shows their coexistence is contingent, not necessary, and this leads to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what would be the consequence if two similars could coexist in one locus?
A) They would both necessarily remain forever.
B) One could cease while the other remains, allowing the locus to be characterized by the opposite of the removed one.
C) They would merge into a single entity.
D) The locus would be destroyed.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that assuming the coexistence of two similars leads to a logical contradiction because one can cease while the other remains, allowing the locus to acquire the opposite of the removed similar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof uses the example of two blacknesses in one locus. Consider your own heart: are there two 'blacknesses'—sins or bad habits—you allow to coexist, assuming you can keep both? What one thing must you remove to avoid logical and spiritual contradiction?
?
The argument shows that if two similars could coexist, one could vanish while the other remains. Are there commitments in your life that you treat as equally valid even though they conflict? Which one is calling you to let it go for the sake of integrity?
?
The proof ends by saying 'twoness is removed.' In your relationship with Allah, is there any 'twoness'—loving Him yet clinging to what He forbids? How will you remove that today?

The Second Argument: Conditional and Clarification
Al-Jurjani states the second argument and clarifies key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second argument against coexistence of similars, specifically the conditional premise and the clarification of 'one thing'.

Core Idea
If two theoretical knowledges could coexist, they would have to be of the same object in a single respect.

Al-Jurjani now presents the second argument against the coexistence of similars, focusing on the case of two theoretical knowledges of the same thing. He begins with a conditional statement: if similars could coexist, then two theoretical knowledges could subsist in one person concerning one thing. This lesson unpacks that conditional and the crucial refinement that 'one thing' means in essence and consideration, not two aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument statement
Start by reading the Arabic sentence that begins with 'والثاني: الإلزام في العلمين النظريين' and ends with 'وهو محال'. This is the core claim: if similars could coexist, two theoretical knowledges of one thing would be possible, which is impossible.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...' which is the form of many kalam arguments.
2
Understand the gloss on 'one thing'
Read the gloss beginning with 'قوله: (بشيء واحد)'. Al-Jurjani clarifies that 'one thing' means in essence and consideration, not two different aspects. This prevents a misunderstanding that the two knowledges might be of different aspects of the same object.
The distinction between 'one thing' absolutely and 'one thing' under two aspects is subtle but essential.
3
Reflect on why the argument is problematic for the opponent
Ask yourself: if the opponent admits that two theoretical knowledges of the same thing in the same respect cannot coexist, then he must also deny the coexistence of similars in general, since theoretical knowledges are a clear case of similars.
This argument is a reductio ad absurdum against the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: الإلزام في العلمين النظريين أي لو جاز اجتماع المثلين لجاز أن يجتمع علمان نظريان بشيء واحد لأنهما مثلان فإذا قام بشخص علم نظري بشيء جاز أن يقوم به أيضاً علم نظري آخر بذلك الشيء وهو محال. قوله: (بشيء واحد) أي بالذات والاعتبار فلا يتجه أنه قد يتصور الشيء بوجهين بالنظر فقد اجتمع العلمان النظريان بشيء واحد. قوله: (إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم) لأن أحد النظرين يكون مقدماً على الآخر لامتناع توجه النفس قصداً إلى شيئين والفرض أن المعلوم شيء واحد بالذات والاعتبار فيلزم أن يكون النظر الثاني في المعلوم من حيث إنه معلوم فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل. قوله: (فلا اثنينية فلا تماثل) بخلاف ما إذا تعاقبا على محل واحد فإن عوارض المحل مختلف في الوقتين. لا يقال لو تم ما ذكره لدل على امتناع عروضهما لمحل واحد بدلاً أيضاً لأنا نقول إذا لم يجتمعا جاز أن يكون للمحل في أحد الزمانين عوارض مخصوصة وفي الزمان الآخر عوارض أخرى فلا تكون نسبة المثلين إلى جميع العوارض نسبة واحدة فجاز امتيازهما بحسب العوارض بخلاف ما لو اجتمعا إذ هاهنا يدعي اتحاد نسبتهما إليها.
And the second: the entailment regarding the two theoretical knowledges, meaning: if the coexistence of similars was permissible, then it would be permissible for two theoretical knowledges to coexist concerning one thing, because they are similars. So if a theoretical knowledge of something subsists in a person, it would be permissible for another theoretical knowledge of that thing to also subsist in him, which is impossible. His saying: (concerning one thing) meaning in essence and consideration, so it is not directed that the thing might be conceptualized in two aspects by reasoning, for then the two theoretical knowledges would have coexisted concerning one thing. His saying: (for reasoning about the known entails) because one of the two reasonings would be prior to the other, due to the impossibility of the soul directing itself intentionally to two things, while the assumption is that the known is one thing in essence and consideration. So it necessarily follows that the second reasoning is about the known inasmuch as it is known, and thus obtaining the obtained is necessitated. His saying: (so no duality, so no similarity) contrary to when they succeed each other in one locus, for the accidents of the locus differ in the two times. It cannot be said: if what he mentioned were valid, it would indicate the impossibility of their occurring in one locus as a substitute as well; for we say: if they do not coexist, it is permissible for the locus to have specific accidents in one time and other accidents in another time, so the relation of the two similars to all accidents is not one and the same, so their differentiation according to accidents is permissible, unlike if they coexisted, for here it is claimed that their relation to them is unified.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the second argument) against the coexistence of two similar things, specifically using the case of two theoretical knowledges. Al-Jurjani begins by stating the argument: if two similars could coexist, then two theoretical knowledges of the same thing could coexist in one person, which is absurd. He then explains the glosses: 'one thing' means in essence and consideration, not two aspects. The entailment of 'reasoning about the known' is that the second reasoning would be about something already known, leading to 'obtaining the obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل). He then clarifies that the impossibility does not apply when the two knowledges occur in succession, because the locus has different accidents at different times. Finally, he addresses an objection: that the argument would also prove that two similars cannot occur in one locus even in succession. The answer is that in succession, the locus has different accidents at different times, allowing differentiation; but when they coexist, the relation to all accidents is one, removing any basis for distinction.

Key Terms
المثلان
العلمان النظريان
الاعتبار

What does 'one thing' (بشيء واحد) mean in the argument?
A) Any object, regardless of aspects
B) One object in essence and consideration
C) One object but possibly with two different aspects
D) Two distinct objects

Key Takeaway
The second argument uses a conditional to show that allowing coexistence of similars leads to the impossible conclusion of two theoretical knowledges of the same thing in the same respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jurjani argues that holding two theoretical knowledges of the same thing in the same respect is impossible. Is there an area in your faith where you entertain two conflicting understandings of a single truth (e.g., Allah's mercy and justice)? How might this argument push you to seek a consistent, coherent understanding rather than allowing comfortable contradictions?
?
The argument relies on the premise that two identical things (similars) cannot coexist in the same subject. Reflect: Do you ever treat similar situations in your life with double standards? Where might you be allowing 'coexistence of similars' in your moral judgments? What would it take to apply the same standard consistently?
?
This lesson shows that even a purely logical argument can have profound implications for belief. How often do you subject your own theological assumptions to rigorous logical scrutiny? Are you willing to abandon a cherished idea if it leads to absurdity? What would that cost you?

The Entailment: Obtaining the Obtained
Al-Jurjani explains the entailment leading to تحصيل الحاصل.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second reasoning about an already known object leads to the absurdity of 'obtaining the obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل).

Core Idea
Two theoretical knowledges of the same thing cannot coexist because the second would be redundant, repeating what is already known.

The core of the second argument lies in demonstrating why two theoretical knowledges of one thing are impossible. Al-Jurjani explains that any second reasoning about the same object would be a repetition of what has already been attained, leading to the logical fallacy of 'obtaining the obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل). This lesson unpacks that entailment and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the entailment passage
Start with the line 'قوله: (إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم)' and read until 'فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل'. This explains why a second reasoning is futile.
Focus on the phrase 'تحصيل الحاصل' – it is a key concept in Islamic logic and theology.
2
Analyze the reasoning
The soul cannot direct attention to two things intentionally at the same time. Since the known is one thing in essence and consideration, the second reasoning must be about that same known as already known, thus obtaining what is already obtained.
This is a temporal argument as well: one reasoning precedes the other.
3
Connect to the conclusion 'no duality, no similarity'
Read the line 'قوله: (فلا اثنينية فلا تماثل)'. Because the second reasoning is impossible, there is no duality of knowledges, and hence no similarity (since similarity requires two distinct things).
This directly refutes the possibility of two similars coexisting in a single locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: الإلزام في العلمين النظريين أي لو جاز اجتماع المثلين لجاز أن يجتمع علمان نظريان بشيء واحد لأنهما مثلان فإذا قام بشخص علم نظري بشيء جاز أن يقوم به أيضاً علم نظري آخر بذلك الشيء وهو محال. قوله: (بشيء واحد) أي بالذات والاعتبار فلا يتجه أنه قد يتصور الشيء بوجهين بالنظر فقد اجتمع العلمان النظريان بشيء واحد. قوله: (إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم) لأن أحد النظرين يكون مقدماً على الآخر لامتناع توجه النفس قصداً إلى شيئين والفرض أن المعلوم شيء واحد بالذات والاعتبار فيلزم أن يكون النظر الثاني في المعلوم من حيث إنه معلوم فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل. قوله: (فلا اثنينية فلا تماثل) بخلاف ما إذا تعاقبا على محل واحد فإن عوارض المحل مختلف في الوقتين. لا يقال لو تم ما ذكره لدل على امتناع عروضهما لمحل واحد بدلاً أيضاً لأنا نقول إذا لم يجتمعا جاز أن يكون للمحل في أحد الزمانين عوارض مخصوصة وفي الزمان الآخر عوارض أخرى فلا تكون نسبة المثلين إلى جميع العوارض نسبة واحدة فجاز امتيازهما بحسب العوارض بخلاف ما لو اجتمعا إذ هاهنا يدعي اتحاد نسبتهما إليها.
And the second: the entailment regarding the two theoretical knowledges, meaning: if the coexistence of similars was permissible, then it would be permissible for two theoretical knowledges to coexist concerning one thing, because they are similars. So if a theoretical knowledge of something subsists in a person, it would be permissible for another theoretical knowledge of that thing to also subsist in him, which is impossible. His saying: (concerning one thing) meaning in essence and consideration, so it is not directed that the thing might be conceptualized in two aspects by reasoning, for then the two theoretical knowledges would have coexisted concerning one thing. His saying: (for reasoning about the known entails) because one of the two reasonings would be prior to the other, due to the impossibility of the soul directing itself intentionally to two things, while the assumption is that the known is one thing in essence and consideration. So it necessarily follows that the second reasoning is about the known inasmuch as it is known, and thus obtaining the obtained is necessitated. His saying: (so no duality, so no similarity) contrary to when they succeed each other in one locus, for the accidents of the locus differ in the two times. It cannot be said: if what he mentioned were valid, it would indicate the impossibility of their occurring in one locus as a substitute as well; for we say: if they do not coexist, it is permissible for the locus to have specific accidents in one time and other accidents in another time, so the relation of the two similars to all accidents is not one and the same, so their differentiation according to accidents is permissible, unlike if they coexisted, for here it is claimed that their relation to them is unified.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the second argument) against the coexistence of two similar things, specifically using the case of two theoretical knowledges. Al-Jurjani begins by stating the argument: if two similars could coexist, then two theoretical knowledges of the same thing could coexist in one person, which is absurd. He then explains the glosses: 'one thing' means in essence and consideration, not two aspects. The entailment of 'reasoning about the known' is that the second reasoning would be about something already known, leading to 'obtaining the obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل). He then clarifies that the impossibility does not apply when the two knowledges occur in succession, because the locus has different accidents at different times. Finally, he addresses an objection: that the argument would also prove that two similars cannot occur in one locus even in succession. The answer is that in succession, the locus has different accidents at different times, allowing differentiation; but when they coexist, the relation to all accidents is one, removing any basis for distinction.

Key Terms
النظر في المعلوم
تحصيل الحاصل
الاثنينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two theoretical knowledges of the same thing could coexist if they occur simultaneously or if the soul can multitask.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that the soul cannot direct itself intentionally to two acts of reasoning at the same time, so one must be prior. Additionally, the object is the same, so the second would be redundant.

Checkpoint
Why is the second reasoning about the known as known considered impossible?
A) Because the soul cannot reason twice about the same thing
B) Because it would entail obtaining what is already obtained (تحصيل الحاصل)
C) Because reasoning must be simultaneous
D) Because the known changes

Key Takeaway
Two theoretical knowledges of one thing cannot coexist because the second would be redundant, resulting in the logical fallacy of 'obtaining the obtained'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a truth about Allah, do you immediately seek to 'obtain' it again through more study, or do you act on what you already know? What truth have you 'obtained' but not lived? Name it and commit to implementing it today.
?
The soul cannot direct itself to two intentions at once. What two competing intentions in your heart prevent you from fully directing your soul to the knowledge of Allah? What will you remove or prioritize to achieve single-focused intention?
?
Is there an area of your faith where you are seeking a 'second knowledge' of the same thing—perhaps through unnecessary debates or over-analysis—while neglecting the practical demands of the first? How will you break this cycle of redundancy and start applying what you know?

Objection and Distinction: Coexistence vs. Succession
Al-Jurjani distinguishes succession from coexistence and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument does not apply when two knowledges occur in succession, and how the distinction based on accidents answers the objection.

Core Idea
Successive knowledges avoid the fallacy because the locus has different accidents at different times, allowing differentiation.

Al-Jurjani anticipates an objection: if the argument shows that two theoretical knowledges cannot coexist, why can they not occur in succession? He clarifies that the impossibility applies only to coexistence, not succession, because the accidents of the locus differ over time. This lesson examines that distinction and the response to the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction passage
Start from 'بخلاف ما إذا تعاقبا على محل واحد' and read to the end. This contrasts coexistence with succession.
Note the phrase 'عوارض المحل مختلف في الوقتين' – the accidents of the locus differ in the two times.
2
Understand the objection
Read 'لا يقال لو تم ما ذكره لدل على امتناع عروضهما لمحل واحد بدلاً أيضاً' – the objection states that the argument would also prevent two knowledges from occurring in the same locus even one after the other.
The opponent is trying to show that the argument proves too much.
3
Analyze the response
Al-Jurjani answers that in succession, the locus has different accidents at each time, so the relation of the similars to all accidents is not uniform. This allows differentiation, which is not possible in coexistence where the relation to accidents is unified.
The key is the unity of 'نسبة المثلين إلى جميع العوارض' in coexistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: الإلزام في العلمين النظريين أي لو جاز اجتماع المثلين لجاز أن يجتمع علمان نظريان بشيء واحد لأنهما مثلان فإذا قام بشخص علم نظري بشيء جاز أن يقوم به أيضاً علم نظري آخر بذلك الشيء وهو محال. قوله: (بشيء واحد) أي بالذات والاعتبار فلا يتجه أنه قد يتصور الشيء بوجهين بالنظر فقد اجتمع العلمان النظريان بشيء واحد. قوله: (إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم) لأن أحد النظرين يكون مقدماً على الآخر لامتناع توجه النفس قصداً إلى شيئين والفرض أن المعلوم شيء واحد بالذات والاعتبار فيلزم أن يكون النظر الثاني في المعلوم من حيث إنه معلوم فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل. قوله: (فلا اثنينية فلا تماثل) بخلاف ما إذا تعاقبا على محل واحد فإن عوارض المحل مختلف في الوقتين. لا يقال لو تم ما ذكره لدل على امتناع عروضهما لمحل واحد بدلاً أيضاً لأنا نقول إذا لم يجتمعا جاز أن يكون للمحل في أحد الزمانين عوارض مخصوصة وفي الزمان الآخر عوارض أخرى فلا تكون نسبة المثلين إلى جميع العوارض نسبة واحدة فجاز امتيازهما بحسب العوارض بخلاف ما لو اجتمعا إذ هاهنا يدعي اتحاد نسبتهما إليها.
And the second: the entailment regarding the two theoretical knowledges, meaning: if the coexistence of similars was permissible, then it would be permissible for two theoretical knowledges to coexist concerning one thing, because they are similars. So if a theoretical knowledge of something subsists in a person, it would be permissible for another theoretical knowledge of that thing to also subsist in him, which is impossible. His saying: (concerning one thing) meaning in essence and consideration, so it is not directed that the thing might be conceptualized in two aspects by reasoning, for then the two theoretical knowledges would have coexisted concerning one thing. His saying: (for reasoning about the known entails) because one of the two reasonings would be prior to the other, due to the impossibility of the soul directing itself intentionally to two things, while the assumption is that the known is one thing in essence and consideration. So it necessarily follows that the second reasoning is about the known inasmuch as it is known, and thus obtaining the obtained is necessitated. His saying: (so no duality, so no similarity) contrary to when they succeed each other in one locus, for the accidents of the locus differ in the two times. It cannot be said: if what he mentioned were valid, it would indicate the impossibility of their occurring in one locus as a substitute as well; for we say: if they do not coexist, it is permissible for the locus to have specific accidents in one time and other accidents in another time, so the relation of the two similars to all accidents is not one and the same, so their differentiation according to accidents is permissible, unlike if they coexisted, for here it is claimed that their relation to them is unified.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the second argument) against the coexistence of two similar things, specifically using the case of two theoretical knowledges. Al-Jurjani begins by stating the argument: if two similars could coexist, then two theoretical knowledges of the same thing could coexist in one person, which is absurd. He then explains the glosses: 'one thing' means in essence and consideration, not two aspects. The entailment of 'reasoning about the known' is that the second reasoning would be about something already known, leading to 'obtaining the obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل). He then clarifies that the impossibility does not apply when the two knowledges occur in succession, because the locus has different accidents at different times. Finally, he addresses an objection: that the argument would also prove that two similars cannot occur in one locus even in succession. The answer is that in succession, the locus has different accidents at different times, allowing differentiation; but when they coexist, the relation to all accidents is one, removing any basis for distinction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument against coexistence also rules out two similar things occurring in succession in the same locus.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies that succession is allowed because the locus has different accidents over time, so the two things are not truly similar in all respects.

Key Takeaway
The argument only disproves coexistence of two theoretical knowledges, not their succession, because the changing accidents of the locus provide a basis for differentiation.

?

?
The objection in the passage reveals a common tendency to overgeneralize a logical argument. In your own life, have you ever rejected a truth because you misunderstood its scope? Will you commit to examining the precise conditions of divine commands before dismissing them?

Addressing the Objection of Two Endpoints
Al-Jurjani raises an objection about two points on a line and answers it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the apparent counterexample of two endpoints of a line to the rule that two similars cannot coexist.

Core Idea
Similar things can coexist if their loci differ in aspect.

The rule that two similars cannot coexist seems to have a clear counterexample: the two endpoints of a line are both points (similar) and yet they exist on the same line. In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses this objection by refining the notion of 'locus'—showing that the loci are not truly identical because they differ in the consideration of termination. This clarifies the rule and deepens our understanding of how distinction is possible without violating the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening phrase 'فإن قلت...' which presents the objection. Notice the claim that two endpoints are similars and that they coexist in a single locus (the line).
The objector is using a physical example to challenge a logical rule.
2
Examine al-Jurjani's first answer
Al-Jurjani responds with 'قلت: أولاً...' pointing out that the objection is based on the principles of philosophers, implying it may not apply within the Kalam framework.
This answer defuses the objection by questioning its premises.
3
Examine the second answer
The second answer ('وثانياً...') is the main resolution: the locus of each point is the same line but with a different consideration—one as termination on one side, the other on the other side. Thus the loci are distinct in modality.
The key term is 'اعتبار' (consideration) which differentiates the otherwise identical loci.
4
Reflect on the final gloss
The passage ends with a note from the commentator about the assumption that two theoretical knowledges cannot arise from a single act of reasoning. This links back to the deeper epistemological principle underlying the rule.
This shows how the objection is embedded in a larger debate about the nature of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت محل كل من النقطتين اللتين هما طرفا خط واحد مجموع ذلك الخط، كما تقرر عندهم، ولا شك أنهما مثلان فقد اجتمع مثلان في محل مع وجود الامتياز بينهما. قلت: أولاً: ما ذكرته مبني على قواعد الفلاسفة، وثانياً: إن محل إحدى النقطتين مجموع الخط باعتبار انتهائه في جانب ومحل النقطة الأخرى ذلك المجموع لكن باعتبار انتهائه في جانب آخر فقد تعدد محلهما بحيثية موجبة لامتياز الحالتين ولا كلام فيه. قوله: (إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم هذا على امتناع حصول المثلين معاً من نظر واحد مبني فليتأمل.
For if you say: The locus of each of the two points that are the endpoints of a single line is the totality of that line, as established with them, and there is no doubt that they are two similars; so two similars have coexisted in a locus along with the existence of distinction between them. I say: First, what you have mentioned is based on the rules of the philosophers; and second, the locus of one of the two points is the totality of the line in respect of its termination on one side, and the locus of the other point is that same totality but in respect of its termination on another side, so their loci have multiplied due to a modality that necessitates the distinction of the two states, and there is no dispute about this. His saying: (for reasoning about the known entails) this is based on the impossibility of the occurrence of two similars together from a single reasoning, so reflect.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the general rule that two similars cannot coexist. The objector argues that the two endpoints of a line are two similars (both are points) and they coexist in the locus of the entire line, yet they are distinct. Al-Jurjani gives a two-part answer. First, this objection relies on the principles of the philosophers, not on sound Islamic premises (perhaps implying a different ontological framework). Second, and more importantly, the locus of each point is the same line but with different considerations: one point is the endpoint on one side, the other on the other side. Thus, the 'locus' is not truly identical; it is multiplied by the modality of termination. Therefore, there is no genuine coexistence of two similars in a single, undifferentiated locus. The final gloss notes that this entire discussion rests on the assumption that two theoretical knowledges cannot arise from a single act of reasoning.

Key Terms
النقطتان
طرفا خط
مجموع الخط
الاعتبار
الحيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two endpoints of a line are a counterexample to the rule that two similars cannot coexist.
Correction: The endpoints are not truly in the same undifferentiated locus because the locus is considered differently for each point (one termination on one side, the other on the other side).

Checkpoint
How does al-Jurjani respond to the objection that two endpoints of a line are similar and coexist in one locus?
A) He rejects the example outright
B) He argues that the locus differs by consideration of the side of termination
C) He says the points are not really similars
D) He admits the rule fails for points

Key Takeaway
The objection is resolved by noting that the locus is the same line but the consideration of termination differs, so the two points are not in the same locus in the same respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a contradiction between a principle you hold and a counterexample, do you immediately abandon the principle or do you search for a deeper consideration (اعتبار) that might reconcile them? What would it mean for you to adopt the patience of the scholars in such moments?
?
The objector relied on the 'rules of the philosophers' without examining their foundation. Are there beliefs or opinions you hold simply because they are common or widely accepted, without verifying their basis? Will you commit to tracing one such belief back to its root this week?
?
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between two aspects of the same locus to resolve the objection. In your own life, do you conflate intentions or perspectives when judging others or yourself? How might your relationships change if you consistently distinguished between different aspects of a person's actions?

The Third Argument: If Two Similars Coexist, One Can Be Removed and Its Opposite Appears
Al-Jurjani presents the third argument that if two similars could coexist, one could be removed and its opposite arise, leading to the impossible coexistence of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's third argument showing that allowing two similars to coexist leads to the impossible coexistence of opposites.

Core Idea
If two like things can coexist, one can vanish and its opposite appear, making opposites coexist.

Al-Jurjani now presents a third argument that targets the possibility of two like things existing together. He shows that if they could, it would lead to the impossible scenario of opposites coexisting. This argument relies on the removal of one similar and the subsequent fitness of the locus for the opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument come through.
Notice that the passage includes a gloss introduced by 'قوله'.
2
Identify the logical steps
The argument proceeds from assuming coexistence is possible, to the removal of one similar, to the locus becoming fit for the opposite, and finally to the contradiction of opposites coexisting. List these steps in order.
3
Understand the gloss
The gloss explains that the removal of one opposite makes the locus receptive to the other opposite, and that opposite is also opposite to the remaining similar. This solidifies the contradiction.
The gloss is clarifying a point about why the opposite can arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه أي الاجتماع على تقدير جوازه لا يجب بحيث يمتنع زواله بعد حصوله فإذا اجتمع سوادان مثلا في محل واحد جاز أن ينتفي عنه أحدهما بقاء الآخر وإذا انتفى عن المحل أحد المثلين فيجوز مع اتصافه بضد المثل المنتفي لأن زوال أحد الضدين عن المحل فإنه يمتنع اجتماعهما بهويتهما إذ لا يجوز الانتقال على شيء منهما حتى يتصور اجتماعهما في محل. قوله: (المحل مصحح لاتصافه بالضد الآخر) وأنه أي ذلك الضد ضد أيضاً له أي للمثل الباقي فيلزم اجتماع السواد الباقي مع ضده هذا خلف.
The third: namely, that coexistence—given its permissibility—is not necessary such that its removal after occurrence is impossible. For if two blacknesses, for example, coexist in one locus, it would be permissible for one of them to be removed from it while the other remains. And when one of the two similars is removed from the locus, then it is permissible for that locus to be qualified by the opposite of the removed similar, because the removal of one of the two opposites from the locus—for it is impossible for them to coexist in their essences—since it is not permissible to transition from something until their coexistence in a locus is imagined. His saying: (the locus makes it fit to be qualified by the other opposite) and that this opposite is also an opposite of the remaining similar, so the coexistence of the remaining blackness with its opposite is necessitated—this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the coexistence of two similars. It argues that if two similars (e.g., two instances of blackness) could coexist in one locus, they would not necessarily persist together forever; one could cease while the other remains. If one blackness disappears, the locus becomes fit to receive its opposite, whiteness. Since black and white are opposites, the remaining blackness would then coexist with whiteness, which is impossible. The gloss clarifies that the removal of one opposite makes the locus receptive to the other opposite, and that opposite is also an opposite of the remaining similar, leading to the impossible coexistence of opposites.

Key Terms
السوادان
الضدان
الزوال
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument assumes that removal of one similar necessarily leads to the opposite arising, but the argument only claims it is possible given the locus's capacity.
Correction: The argument does not require that the opposite must arise; it only says that if it is possible for one similar to vanish, then it is also possible for its opposite to appear, and that possibility is enough to derive a contradiction because the opposite would clash with the remaining similar.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude would happen if two like things could coexist in one locus?
A) They would remain together forever
B) One could be removed, allowing its opposite to arise, leading to coexistence of opposite and the remaining similar
C) They would become distinct
D) The locus would be destroyed

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that allowing coexistence of similars leads to the absurdity of opposites coexisting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever try to hold two contradictory beliefs at the same time? What is the 'impossible combination' you are tolerating, and how can you honestly resolve it using the clarity this argument demands?
?
This argument shows that if we allow one impossibility, it leads to another. Is there a small compromise in your faith or actions that you have allowed, thinking it harmless, which actually sets up an inevitable contradiction with what you truly believe? What will you do about it now?
?
The argument uses a logical reductio to force a conclusion. When have you avoided following an argument to its end because you feared the conclusion? What would it cost you to be consistent with the truth you already know?

The Fourth Argument: Loss of Certainty About a Single Blackness
Al-Jurjani presents the fourth argument: if two similars could coexist, we could not be certain of a single blackness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against the coexistence of similars, which shows that such a possibility would undermine our intuitive certainty about simple sensory judgments.

Core Idea
If two similars could coexist, we could not be sure that a blackness is one.

Al-Jurjani now presents a fourth argument that appeals to immediate intuitive certainty. We are naturally certain that when we see a black object, the blackness in it is a single instance. If it were possible for two similar blacknesses to coexist in the same place, that certainty would be lost. This argument highlights how even our most basic sensory judgments would become unreliable if the principle against the coexistence of similars were violated.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that it is very concise: a conditional statement and a conclusion, followed by a gloss.
The gloss clarifies that the permissibility of coexistence removes safety from an intuitive judgment.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument is: If coexistence of similars were allowed (premise), then we could not be certain that a specific blackness is one (conclusion). But we are in fact certain of such judgments (implied premise). Therefore, coexistence is not allowed.
This is a modus tollens argument: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
3
Reflect on the concept of intuitive certainty
Consider what 'intuitive certainty' (بديهة) means. It is immediate knowledge that does not require proof. The argument relies on the fact that we have such certainty about simple sensory judgments like 'this blackness is one.'
If that certainty is lost, a foundational category of knowledge would be undermined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع لو جاز اجتماع المثلين لم يمكنا الجزم بأن القائم بالمحل المعين سواد واحد لكنا بذلك نجزم. قوله: (لو جاز إلخ) خلاصته أن الجواز المذكور يستلزم رفع الأمان عن الحكم المعلوم بالبديهة.
The fourth: if the coexistence of similars were permissible, we would not be able to be certain that what subsists in a specific locus is a single blackness, whereas we are certain of that. His saying: (if it were permissible, etc.) its summary is that the aforementioned permissibility entails removing safety from the judgment known by intuition.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, which is based on intuitive certainty. We are certain that when we see a black object, there is a single blackness in it. If it were possible for two separate blacknesses to coexist in the same locus, we could not be sure that what we perceive is one blackness rather than two. This would undermine the intuitive certainty we have about such basic perceptible judgments. The gloss summarizes: the permissibility of coexistence would remove the safety (i.e., reliability) from a judgment known by intuition (بديهة).

Key Terms
الجزم
السواد الواحد
البديهة
الأمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that even if two blacknesses could coexist, we could still be certain by using other methods, such as measuring the intensity of blackness or relying on other senses.
Correction: The argument targets intuitive certainty itself—the immediate, unreflective judgment that this blackness is one. Such certainty is basic and does not admit of further verification; if it could be false due to a metaphysical possibility, then its status as certain is undermined. The argument shows that allowing coexistence would erode the foundation of a whole class of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what would be lost if similar things could coexist?
A) The ability to distinguish colors
B) The certainty that a particular blackness is one instance
C) The existence of blackness entirely
D) The need for proof

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument shows that allowing the coexistence of similars would destroy our intuitive certainty about basic sensory judgments, which is unacceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at this black text on a white screen, are you absolutely certain it is one continuous blackness? What would it mean for your daily life if that certainty were undermined by the possibility of two identical blacknesses coexisting? How does this lesson challenge you to trust your intuitive knowledge about more profound matters, like the oneness of Allah?
?
Is there an intuitive certainty in your heart—something you know without proof—that you have allowed doubt to creep into? What would it cost you to restore that certainty by rejecting the 'coexistence of similars' in that realm? Commit to one specific step to reclaim that certainty today.
?
The argument says that allowing similar possibilities 'removes safety from judgment.' Think of a belief about your faith that you hold with absolute certainty. Are you protecting that belief from intellectual 'safety removal' by accepting alternative possibilities that contradict it? If so, are you willing to follow al-Jurjani's reasoning and reject those possibilities?

Critique of the First Argument: Distinction and Ignorance
Critique of the first argument for the impossibility of coexistence of similars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Jurjani considers the first argument for the impossibility of coexistence of similars to be open to criticism, particularly regarding the distinction between actual lack of distinction and ignorance of distinction.

Core Idea
Ignorance of distinction does not prove actual indistinction.

Al-Jurjani begins his critical analysis of the four arguments by examining the first. He points out that the lack of apparent distinction between two similars does not entail their actual indistinction, because they could be distinguished by accidents from separate causes. Moreover, our inability to perceive a distinction is not a logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening critique
Read the first part of the passage where Al-Jurjani introduces the critique of all paths and then focuses on the first.
The first argument is not fully quoted; only the critique is given.
2
Understand the objection
Notice the two points: (1) actual distinction is possible via separate accidents, and (2) our lack of knowledge of distinction is not impossible.
3
Reflect on its significance
Ask: How does this critique undermine the claim that similars cannot coexist? It shows that the argument confuses epistemic and ontological indistinction.
This connects to the broader kalam debate on the nature of similars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيها أي في هذه المسالك كلها نظر، فالأول منظور فيه؛ إذ عدم التمايز في نفس الأمر ممتنع؛ لجواز تمايز المثلين عند الاجتماع بعوارض مستندة إلى أسباب مفارقة دون المحل، وعدم التمايز عندنا غير ممتنع؛ مرجعه عدم علمنا بالتمايز ولا محذور فيه. وكذا الثاني منظور فيه، لأنه لا يوجب السلب الكلي الذي هو المدعى أعني قولنا: لا يجوز اجتماع المثلين أصلاً، بل يوجب سلب الكل؛ لأن امتناع اجتماع هذين المثلين أعني العلمين النظريين المتعلقين بمعلوم واحد يوجب رفع الإيجاب الكلي، أعني قولنا: ليس. قوله: ( الثالث إلخ) فيه بحث: لأن هذا الدليل مشترك الإلزام لأن العرض لا يبقى زمانين عند أهل الحق، بل بقاؤه بتجدد الأمثال، فانتفاء مثل واحد يصحح طرو ضده على محله الطارئ عليه مثل آخر فيجتمع الضدان، على أنه لو صح أن زوال أحد الضدين على المحل مصحح لاتصافه بالضد الآخر، لصح أن انتفاء أحد الضدين في محل قابل لذاته مصحح الآخر، وإلا فلا بد من الفرق بين الانتفاء بعد الوجود، أعني الزوال، والانتفاء مطلقا وبين القابلية الذاتية. فانتفاء المثل في محل المثل الآخر مصحح لطرو ضده المستلزم لاجتماع الضدين، فتأمل. قوله: ( الرابع لو جاز إلخ) قيل: هذا من لوازم المسلك الأول، ولهذا لما ذكر الإمام الأول لم يذكر هذا، والآمدي لما ذكر هذا لم يذكر الأول. قوله: ( إلى أسباب مفارقة ) أما الفاعل المختار المميز بإرادته كلاً من المثلين بما يخصه من العوارض المتخالفة مع الاشتراك فيما ذكر، وأما الفواعل لا بالاختيار التي بين أحدها وأحد المثلين مناسبة مخصوصة، فإن ذلك جائز، كما مر في بحث التعين. قوله: ( وكذا الثاني منظور فيه ) قد يجاب عن هذا النظر بأن ما ذكره ليس دليلاً على المدعى، بل هو نقض كلام الخصم كما يشعر به لفظ الإلزام، وكفى بصورة واحدة نقضاً. ولهذا قال الآمدي فيه: وهذا المسلك قوي جداً، وهذا مبني على أن مدعى الخصم هو الإيجاب الكلي، وستعرف ما فيه.
And in them—i.e., in all these paths—there is consideration. So the first: it is open to consideration, for the lack of distinction in actual fact is impossible, because it is permissible for the two similars to be distinguished at the time of coexistence by accidents based on separate causes apart from the locus, and lack of distinction with us is not impossible; its reference is our lack of knowledge of distinction and there is no harm in it. And likewise the second is open to consideration, because it does not necessitate the universal negation that is the claim, namely our saying: it is not permissible for similars to coexist at all, but rather it necessitates the negation of the universal, because the impossibility of the coexistence of these two similars, namely the two theoretical knowledges related to one known, necessitates the removal of the universal affirmation, namely our saying: not. His saying: (the third, etc.) there is discussion in it, because this proof is common in its entailment, for an accident does not persist for two moments according to the people of truth, rather its persistence is through the renewal of likes, so the removal of one like makes it correct for its opposite to occur in its locus upon which another like occurs, thus the two opposites coexist. Moreover, if it were valid that the removal of one of the two opposites from the locus makes it fit to be qualified by the other opposite, then it would be valid that the non-existence of one of the two opposites in a locus that is receptive by itself makes it fit for the other, otherwise there must be a difference between non-existence after existence, i.e., removal, and non-existence absolutely, and between intrinsic receptivity. So the non-existence of a like in the locus of another like makes it fit for the occurrence of its opposite, necessitating the coexistence of the two opposites, so reflect. His saying: (the fourth, if it were permissible, etc.) it was said: this is one of the consequences of the first path, and for this reason when Imam (al-Razi) mentioned the first he did not mention this, and al-Amidi when he mentioned this he did not mention the first. His saying: (to separate causes) as for the free agent who distinguishes by his will each of the two similars with the specific accidents that are different despite the commonality in what was mentioned, and as for non-volitional agents between one of them and one of the two similars there is a specific suitability, then that is permissible, as has passed in the discussion of specification. His saying: (and likewise the second is open to consideration) it may be answered about this consideration: that what he mentioned is not a proof for the claim, but rather it is a refutation of the opponent's statement, as the word 'entailment' suggests, and one single instance is sufficient as a refutation. For this reason al-Amidi said about it: This path is very strong, and this is based on the claim of the opponent being the universal affirmation, and you will know what is in it.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination (نظر) of the four arguments presented earlier. It begins by stating that all these paths are open to consideration. For the first argument (not fully present in our window), the critique is that lack of distinction in actual fact is not necessary; the two similars could be distinguished by accidents from separate causes, and our ignorance of distinction is not problematic. For the second argument, the critique is that it does not prove the universal negation (that no two similars can ever coexist) but only negates the universal affirmation (that they always coexist); it is a negative proof rather than a positive one. For the third argument, the critique is deeper: according to the truth (i.e., the Ash'ari view), accidents do not persist over two moments but are continuously recreated. So the removal of one like allows the opposite to occur, but then another like might arise, leading to the coexistence of opposites. The argument also fails to distinguish between removal after existence and absolute non-existence, and between intrinsic and accidental receptivity. For the fourth argument, it is noted that it is a corollary of the first argument, and the scholars differ on its independence. Additional glosses clarify the meaning of 'separate causes' and offer a defense of the second argument: that it is not a proof but a refutation (نقض) of the opponent's claim, and al-Amidi considered it very strong.

Key Terms
التمايز
الجواز
العوارض
الأسباب المفارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things appear identical and we cannot distinguish them, they must be the same thing.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that we may simply be ignorant of the distinction; actual distinction could exist due to accidental properties from separate causes.

Checkpoint
According to the critique of the first argument, why is 'lack of distinction in actual fact' not impossible?
A) Because similars always have distinct essences
B) Because they could be distinguished by accidents from separate causes
C) Because the locus itself distinguishes them
D) Because distinction is unknowable

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani shows that the first argument fails because it confuses the lack of perceived distinction with actual lack of distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been convinced of a theological position simply because you couldn't imagine an alternative? What does Al-Jurjani's critique of the first argument reveal about the danger of mistaking your own ignorance for proof? How will you guard against this in your study of 'Aqeedah?
?
Think of a time you assumed two similar things were identical because you saw no difference between them. How does this passage challenge you to reconsider your tendency to judge based on what you perceive? What practical step can you take today to investigate deeper rather than assume?
?
Al-Jurjani points out that lack of distinction 'with us' (i.e., in our knowledge) is not impossible. When have you been guilty of equating your lack of understanding with the non-existence of something in reality? How can you cultivate intellectual humility in light of this?

7 min
Critique of the Second Argument: Negation of the Universal vs Universal Negation
Critique of the second argument from the example of two knowledges.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Jurjani's critique of the second argument focuses on its conclusion: it only negates the universal affirmation, not establishes a universal negation.

Core Idea
A proof that some similars cannot coexist does not prove all cannot.

The second argument attempts to prove that no two similars can ever coexist. Al-Jurjani points out that the argument, even if sound, only shows that certain specific similars (like two theoretical knowledges of the same object) cannot coexist, not that all similars are impossible to coexist. The distinction between 'negation of the universal' and 'universal negation' is critical here.

Guided Path
1
Read the short critique
Read Al-Jurjani's statement on the second argument: it does not necessitate the universal negation (السلب الكلي) but only negation of the universal (سلب الكل).
The Arabic wording is tricky: 'لا يوجب السلب الكلي بل يوجب سلب الكل'.
2
Understand the distinction
Learn the logical distinction: 'Universal negation' means 'No similars can coexist'; 'Negation of the universal' means 'It is not the case that all similars can coexist' or 'Some similars cannot coexist.'
This is a standard logical move.
3
Apply it to the example
The argument uses the example of two theoretical knowledges of the same thing. Even if they cannot coexist, that doesn't prove that all pairs of similars (e.g., two blacknesses) cannot coexist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If one type of similar cannot coexist, then no similars can coexist.
Correction: Al-Jurjani points out this is a logical fallacy: from a specific case you cannot infer a universal rule without additional premises.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Jurjani say the second argument actually proves?
A) The universal negation (السلب الكلي)
B) The negation of the universal (سلب الكل)
C) The universal affirmation (الإيجاب الكلي)
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani exposes the logical gap between proving that some similars cannot coexist and proving that all similars cannot.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed an Islamic teaching because one example of it seemed flawed? This lesson warns that a single counterexample does not disprove a general principle. Will you commit to examining the full evidence before rejecting a belief?
?
In your spiritual life, do you ever assume that because one act of worship felt empty, all acts of worship are futile? That is the logical error of universal negation. How can you remind yourself that 'not all' is not 'none'? What practical step will you take this week to avoid such sweeping despair?

Critique of the Third Argument: The Problem of Renewed Accidents
Critique of the third argument highlighting the consequences of the renewal of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's objection to the third argument, which relies on the Ash'ari doctrine of the non-persistence of accidents and the renewal of likes.

Core Idea
If accidents are continuously renewed, the third argument's reasoning can backfire.

The third argument attempts to prove the impossibility of coexistence of similars by considering the removal of one similar making way for its opposite. Al-Jurjani critiques it from the Ash'ari perspective that accidents do not persist but are renewed moment by moment. This leads to a scenario where the removal of one like might allow another like to appear, causing opposites to coexist, which is the very conclusion the argument tried to avoid.

Guided Path
1
Read the third critique
Read the long critique of the third argument, focusing on the points about renewal of accidents and the problem of non-existence after existence vs absolute non-existence.
This is the densest part.
2
Identify the Ash'ari premise
Note that the critique assumes the Ash'ari view that accidents do not persist over two moments (they are recreated).
This is a key theological difference.
3
Understand the objection
If an accident is removed, another 'like' could appear. That would allow the opposite to arise as well, leading to coexistence of opposites. The argument fails to distinguish between removal after existence (زوال) and absolute non-existence.
Also note the distinction between intrinsic receptivity (قابلية ذاتية) and accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination (نظر) of the four arguments presented earlier. It begins by stating that all these paths are open to consideration. For the first argument (not fully present in our window), the critique is that lack of distinction in actual fact is not necessary; the two similars could be distinguished by accidents from separate causes, and our ignorance of distinction is not problematic. For the second argument, the critique is that it does not prove the universal negation (that no two similars can ever coexist) but only negates the universal affirmation (that they always coexist); it is a negative proof rather than a positive one. For the third argument, the critique is deeper: according to the truth (i.e., the Ash'ari view), accidents do not persist over two moments but are continuously recreated. So the removal of one like allows the opposite to occur, but then another like might arise, leading to the coexistence of opposites. The argument also fails to distinguish between removal after existence and absolute non-existence, and between intrinsic and accidental receptivity. For the fourth argument, it is noted that it is a corollary of the first argument, and the scholars differ on its independence. Additional glosses clarify the meaning of 'separate causes' and offer a defense of the second argument: that it is not a proof but a refutation (نقض) of the opponent's claim, and al-Amidi considered it very strong.

Key Terms
الأمثال
الزوال
القابلية الذاتية
الضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third argument is a straightforward proof that similars cannot coexist.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that when combined with the Ash'ari view of accident renewal, the argument actually may lead to the opposite conclusion.

Checkpoint
According to the critique, why does the third argument 'backfire'?
A) It assumes accidents persist
B) It relies on the renewal of likes
C) It confuses universal and particular
D) It is a corollary of the first argument

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani uses the Ash'ari doctrine of accident renewal to undermine the third argument, showing it can lead to the coexistence of opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jurjani uncovers a hidden contradiction in a seemingly logical argument. When was the last time you confidently defended a belief without examining its underlying assumptions? What would it take for you to apply his 'common entailment' scrutiny to your own thinking today?
?
The distinction between 'removal after existence' and 'absolute non-existence' is crucial in this critique. In your spiritual life, are you prone to confusing temporary shortcomings with a permanent absence of virtue? How can this distinction help you repent meaningfully rather than despair?
?
The passage ends with 'so reflect' (فتأمل). What is one deeply held assumption about your faith or practice that you have never critically examined? Will you set aside time this week to test it against revelation and reason, as Al-Jurjani tested the argument?

Critique of the Fourth Argument and Defense of the Second
Comments on the fourth argument and a defense of the second argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's remarks on the fourth argument as a corollary of the first, and the defense of the second argument as a refutation rather than a proof.

Core Idea
The fourth argument is dependent on the first, and the second argument might be a valid refutation.

The passage ends with comments on the fourth argument and a response to the critique of the second. Al-Jurjani notes that the fourth argument is a consequence of the first, which is why some scholars (like al-Razi and al-Amidi) did not include both. He also presents a possible defense of the second argument: that it is not a proof but a refutation (نقض) of the opponent's claim, and as such a single instance is sufficient. Al-Amidi considered this path very strong.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth critique
Read the brief statement: the fourth is a corollary of the first, and scholars differed on including it.
This is more a historical observation.
2
Read the defense of the second argument
Al-Jurjani reports that some responded to the earlier critique by saying the argument is not a proof but a refutation.
Note the word 'إلزام' (entailment) indicates it's a refutation.
3
Understand the significance
This shows that the same argument can be viewed differently; what one calls a proof another calls a refutation.
Al-Amidi's stance is mentioned as 'very strong'.

And in them—i.e., in all these paths—there is consideration. So the first: it is open to consideration, for the lack of distinction in actual fact is impossible, because it is permissible for the two similars to be distinguished at the time of coexistence by accidents based on separate causes apart from the locus, and lack of distinction with us is not impossible; its reference is our lack of knowledge of distinction and there is no harm in it. And likewise the second is open to consideration, because it does not necessitate the universal negation that is the claim, namely our saying: it is not permissible for similars to coexist at all, but rather it necessitates the negation of the universal, because the impossibility of the coexistence of these two similars, namely the two theoretical knowledges related to one known, necessitates the removal of the universal affirmation, namely our saying: not. His saying: (the third, etc.) there is discussion in it, because this proof is common in its entailment, for an accident does not persist for two moments according to the people of truth, rather its persistence is through the renewal of likes, so the removal of one like makes it correct for its opposite to occur in its locus upon which another like occurs, thus the two opposites coexist. Moreover, if it were valid that the removal of one of the two opposites from the locus makes it fit to be qualified by the other opposite, then it would be valid that the non-existence of one of the two opposites in a locus that is receptive by itself makes it fit for the other, otherwise there must be a difference between non-existence after existence, i.e., removal, and non-existence absolutely, and between intrinsic receptivity. So the non-existence of a like in the locus of another like makes it fit for the occurrence of its opposite, necessitating the coexistence of the two opposites, so reflect. His saying: (the fourth, if it were permissible, etc.) it was said: this is one of the consequences of the first path, and for this reason when Imam (al-Razi) mentioned the first he did not mention this, and al-Amidi when he mentioned this he did not mention the first. For this reason al-Amidi said about it: This path is very strong, and this is based on the claim of the opponent being the universal affirmation, and you will know what is in it.

The Third Point: Emptiness Argument and Its Refutation
Refutes the third argument by denying both its premises and answering an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite argument based on emptiness and its opposite is rejected in Kalam, and how the author refutes both premises and answers an objection.

Core Idea
Empty of both a thing and its opposite is possible, so the argument fails.

This lesson examines the third argument of the Mu'tazilites, which attempts to prove the possibility of combining opposites by assuming emptiness. We will see how the author refutes both premises and then answers an objection, clarifying a key point about the eligibility for an attribute.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument's structure
Identify the two premises that the third argument rests on: (1) the subject can be empty of one of two similar things, (2) the subject cannot be empty of both a thing and its opposite.
The argument aims to show that combining opposites is possible.
2
Examine the refutation of the first premise
The first premise is denied because the two similar things might be necessary (lāzim) to the subject, so neither can depart.
Think of attributes like heat in fire: both similar aspects may be inseparable.
3
Examine the refutation of the second premise
The second premise is denied because the subject could be empty of both the vanishing similar and its opposite, so no contradiction arises.
Example: a celestial body is neither heavy nor light.
4
Follow the objection and response
The opponent objects that absence of one similar makes the subject eligible for its opposite, implying possible combination. The author responds that the remaining similar prevents that eligibility.
Note the technical term 'muṣaḥḥiḥ' (rendering capable).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كذا الثالث منظور فيه؛ لأنه فرع جواز الخلو أي خلو المحل الذي اجتمع فيه المثلان عن أحدهما، وفرع أن المحل لا يخلو عن الشيء وضده، وكلاهما ممنوع، أما الأول فلجواز أن يكون المثلان المجتمعان في محل لازمين له، فلا يجوز زوال شيء منهما عنه، وأما الثاني: فلجواز أن يخلو المحل عن الشيء الذي هو المثل الزائل وعن ضده أيضاً، فلا يلزم اجتماع الضدين. فإن قلت: نحن نقول أن انتفاء أحد المثلين عن المحل يصحح اتصافه بضده فيلزم جواز اجتماع المتضادين قطعاً، ولا حاجة بنا إلى وقوعه. قلت: لا نسلم أيضاً كون ذلك الانتفاء مصححاً للضد؛ وجود المثل الباقي.
And likewise the third [point] is under scrutiny; because it is based on the possibility of emptiness—i.e., the subject in which the two similar things have combined being empty of one of them—and based on the subject not being empty of a thing and its opposite. Both are denied. As for the first, because it is possible that the two similar things combined in a subject are necessary to it, so it is not permissible for any of them to depart from it. As for the second, because it is possible that the subject is empty of the thing that is the vanishing similar and also of its opposite, so the combination of two opposites does not necessarily occur. If you say: We assert that the absence of one of the two similar things from the subject renders it capable of being described by its opposite, thus the possibility of combining two opposites necessarily follows, and we have no need of its actual occurrence. I say: We also do not accept that that absence renders it capable of the opposite, due to the existence of the remaining similar.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (المسلك الثالث) of the Mu'tazilites and its refutation. The author states that the third point is also under scrutiny because it rests on two premises: (1) that the subject (محل) in which the two similar things exist can be empty of one of them, and (2) that the subject cannot be empty of a thing and its opposite. Both premises are rejected. The rejection of the first: the two similar things might be necessary (لازم) to the subject, so they cannot depart. The rejection of the second: the subject might be empty of both the vanishing similar and its opposite, so the conjunction of opposites does not necessarily follow. The opponent then raises an objection: even if the actual combination of opposites does not occur, the absence of one similar makes the subject eligible (مصحح) for its opposite, which implies the possibility of combining opposites. The author answers: we do not accept that the absence makes the subject eligible for the opposite, because the remaining similar exists.

Key Terms
ثالث
فرع
جواز الخلو
محل
لازم
ضد
انتفاء
مصحح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a subject becomes empty of one similar, it must necessarily be described by its opposite.
Correction: The passage shows that emptiness of both is possible (e.g., celestial body is neither heavy nor light), and even if one similar is absent, the remaining similar may prevent the opposite.

Checkpoint
Why is the second premise of the third argument denied?
A) Because the subject cannot be empty of both a thing and its opposite
B) Because the subject is necessarily occupied by one of the two similars
C) Because the subject may be empty of both the vanishing similar and its opposite
D) Because the two similars must coexist

Key Takeaway
The third argument fails because both its premises are false: the subject can be empty of both the vanishing similar and its opposite, and the two similars may be inherent to the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you often assume that a situation must be either one way or its opposite, ignoring the possibility that both could be absent? Where in your life right now are you forcing a binary choice when a third option exists? Commit to identifying one such area today and exploring the middle ground.
?
The author denies that absence makes the opposite possible because of the remaining similar. Think of a bad habit or flaw you've tried to remove – what 'remaining similar' (a coexisting belief, attachment, or flaw) prevents its opposite virtue from taking root? Will you confront that remaining factor this week?
?
The third argument assumes that if one similar can vanish, its opposite must be possible. In your relationship with Allah, do you have any expectations that because He gives, He must take away? Or because He shows mercy, He must also show wrath? How does this lesson's logic of 'both may be absent' free you from that entitlement? What change in your du'a or expectation will you make today?

Objection and Answer: Succession Refutes Coexistence
Objection and direct answer from the commentator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dyeing example does not prove that two similar colours can coexist, and how the principle of succession resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Colours succeed each other; they do not meet.

The opponent argues that dyeing shows two similar colours meeting. But the answer is simpler: each shade replaces the previous one. Let's examine the objection and the key response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection that uses repeated dipping to claim that two similar colours (like blackness) can coexist.
Look for the terms kudrah, kahbah, hulūkah.
2
Study the answer
The answer states that colours occur successively, not simultaneously.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the objector was mistaken. How does succession uphold the principle?

Source Text

The Fourth Observatory - The Tenth Objective: Every Two Similars Do Not Meet. And that difference in its colour due to repeated dipping is only because of the multiplication of the individuals of absolute blackness upon it. So the dull black (kahbah) is two turbidities (kudratayn), and when they combine with blackness they become two dull blacks (kahbatān), and the deep black (hulūkah) is two blacknesses. So the meeting of two similars is established. (The answer: that each one of them, i.e., of the mentioned colours, is a colour differing from the other in intensity and weakness, and these colours occur upon the body in succession, and by the second the first is removed from it, and their meeting is not conceivable in that body at all. However, because the later is more intense than the earlier in blackness, it was imagined that there is a meeting of two similar colours. His statement: (except for) the multiplication etc. The exclusivity is denied; because it is possible that the difference is due to the body's differing in accepting the dye particles, or due to the difference of the dye particles in dyeing. His statement: (The answer that each one of them etc.) This is the truth. For the different colours in the case of the transformation of fruits from greenness to blackness occur in succession, and likewise in the case of dyeing. Therefore he did not answer with the two previous preventives. Blackness in 'Amr, along with the impossibility of their meeting, so they do not claim absolute affirmation at all, but rather partial affirmation, so their evidence agrees with their claim. As for making it in the force of prevention, it is an apparent artificiality that the speaker committed, not out of necessity, while the wording 'for them' and the statement of the commentator in his explanation in proving its permissibility indicate its corruption. It may be said: what is meant by the mentioned evidence is proving absolute permissibility, and its summary: that which is essential is not removed by another, so if the obstacle were the essence of the two similars, they would not have met in this case, so it is established that there is no essential obstacle, so essential absolute permissibility is established. There is in it: that the impossibility of meeting according to those who claim it is also not due to their essences, and hence the two accidental similars were excluded from the definition of contraries by this restriction as the commentator mentioned. So it is permissible that meeting is impossible in some places due to a specificity not found in another. His statement: (turbidity) is the opposite of clarity, and dull black (kahbah) is a colour not pure in redness, and it is specifically in redness, and something becomes deeply black (hulūkah) i.e., its blackness intensifies. His statement: (except for the multiplication of the individuals of blackness) it is said: rather the truth is that small parts of the dye are inserted, then like it, then like it, and there is distance in it because it is a denial of the occurrence of blackness in reality and that it is stubbornness. It may be said: rather some parts become coloured, then another, then another, and there is also distance in it. His statement: (and blackness is two dull blacks) the three turbidities: when each of its second and third join the first, two dull blacks are obtained, and there is no need for four turbidities as is imagined. His statement: (and by the second the first is removed) for example, the rank that deserved the name of turbidity was removed in the second dipping, and due to its becoming strong, another rank was obtained that deserved by its specificity another name, and so on, not that the dye obtained in the first ranks was removed in its second.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of objections against the principle that two similar things cannot meet (coexist). The objector uses an example from dyeing: when a cloth is repeatedly dipped, the colour appears to change in shades, suggesting that two similar colours (e.g., two blacknesses) have met. The answer follows: the colours are not simultaneous but successive; each new shade replaces the previous one. The commentator elaborates on the response, rejecting the exclusivity claim that the colour change is due solely to multiplication of blackness individuals, and instead offers alternative explanations (e.g., differences in the body's receptivity or the dye particles). He also explains why the objector's evidence only proves partial, not absolute, permissibility of meeting. The passage concludes with lexical clarifications and a detailed account of how colours succeed each other, emphasizing that the dye from earlier stages is not preserved but replaced.

Key Terms
متماثلين
اجتماع
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dyeing example proves that two similar colours can coexist at the same time.
Correction: The colours are successive; each new shade replaces the previous one, so they do not meet simultaneously.

Checkpoint
According to the response, how do the colours in the dyeing example relate to each other?
A) They coexist simultaneously.
B) They occur in succession, each replacing the previous.
C) They are identical in intensity.
D) They are actually the same colour.

Key Takeaway
The dyeing example does not refute the impossibility of two similars meeting because the colours are successive, not simultaneous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The objector used a real-world example to challenge a principle of reason. When was the last time you let a vivid example override a clear rational principle? Will you now commit to testing examples more carefully before letting them overturn what you know to be true?
