Critique and Refinement: Absolute vs Partial Permissibility
Commentator's critique of the objection and discussion of permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector's argument only suggests partial permissibility, and how the commentator rejects the absolute exclusivity claim.

Core Idea
The objection proves only partial, not absolute, permissibility.

The commentator further examines the objection and its assumptions. He shows that the exclusivity claim (that colour change is only due to multiplication) is not necessary, and that the objector's evidence only supports partial permissibility. Let's delve into this deeper analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exclusivity claim
The objector claims the colour change is only due to multiplication of blackness individuals. The commentator rejects this.
2
Understand the alternative explanations
The commentator suggests other reasons: the body's receptivity or dye particle differences.
3
Grasp the distinction between absolute and partial
The objector's evidence only proves that meeting can occur in some cases, not always. This is partial permissibility.

Two Answers: Virtue and Vice are Not Opposite Genera
The author gives two answers to the claim that virtue/vice and good/evil are opposite genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two distinct answers to the objection that virtue and vice, like good and evil, are opposite genera.

Core Idea
Virtue and vice are not opposite genera; their opposition is privative or accidental.

This passage addresses a potential misconception: that virtue and vice are opposite genera, similar to good and evil. The author provides two clear answers to show why this opposition is not essential. One answer uses the category of privation and possession; the other treats the opposition as accidental. Let's examine the source to see how these arguments unfold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice that the passage presents two answers introduced by 'first' and 'second'.
2
Identify the two answers
Pick out the two answers: (1) the category of privation and possession, (2) accidental opposition. Understand how each answer resolves the objection.
The first answer defines vice as privation of virtue; the second says the opposition is in the accidents, not the genera.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on the terms 'العدم والملكة' (privation and possession) and 'التضاد بالعرض' (accidental opposition). See how these terms reframe the discussion.
These are technical terms in kalam and logic; understanding them is crucial.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why it matters that virtue and vice are not opposite genera. This protects against a false assumption about moral concepts.
The author is careful to avoid conflating essential and accidental properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احب الكتاب أن الفضيلة والرذيلة أيضاً جنسان بينهما تضاد كالخير والشر، ثم أشار إلى الجواب أولاً بأن الكل من قبيل العدم والملكة فإن الرذيلة عدم الفضيلة كما أن الشرية عدم الخيرية. وثانياً بأن التضاد في الكل بالعرض؛ أي هذه الأمور الأربعة أمور عارضة ليس شيء منها جنساً لما تحته على قياس ما عرفت، فكون الشيء خيراً ضد لكونه شراً، كما أن كونه فضيلة ضد لكونه رذيلة، فلم يثبت تضاد بين الأجناس بل بين العوارض التي يجوز أن يكون كل متضادين منها تحت جنس واحد.
The author holds that virtue and vice are also two genera between which there is opposition like good and evil. Then he pointed to the answer first that all of them are of the category of privation and possession, for vice is the privation of virtue just as evilness is the privation of goodness. And second, that opposition in all is accidental, i.e., these four matters are accidental, none of them is a genus for what is under it on the pattern of what you have known. So the thing's being good is opposite to its being evil, just as its being a virtue is opposite to its being a vice. Thus opposition between genera is not established, but rather between the accidents, each pair of which may fall under a single genus.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the two answers to an objection. The objection is that virtue and vice, like good and evil, seem to be genera that oppose each other. The first answer uses the category of privation and possession: vice is the absence of virtue, just as evil is the absence of good. This means the opposition is not between independent realities but between having and lacking. The second answer treats the opposition as accidental: the attributes of being virtuous or vicious, good or evil are accidents that happen to things. The real opposition is between these accidental qualities, not between the genera themselves. Therefore, no opposition exists between genera as such, only between the accidents that can be subsumed under a single genus.

Key Terms
العدم والملكة
التضاد بالعرض
الفضيلة والرذيلة
الخير والشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Virtue and vice are opposite genera like good and evil.
Correction: They are not; their opposition is either privative (vice is absence of virtue) or accidental (opposition in accidents, not in genera).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first answer given to the objection that virtue and vice are opposite genera?
A) They are both under a common genus.
B) Vice is the privation of virtue.
C) They are not real opposites.
D) They are only accidentally opposed in a single genus.

Key Takeaway
Virtue and vice are not opposite genera; their opposition is either privative or accidental, not essential.

The Principle: A Single Real Opposite Has Only One Opposite
The author asserts the principle and provides induction-based examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single real opposite can only have one opposite and how this is observed in attributes like courage and its extremes.

Core Idea
A real opposite is always a single extreme, not a middle virtue.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle in philosophical theology: real opposites are binary. Using the example of courage, the author demonstrates that virtues like courage are not directly opposite to their extremes but rather the extremes oppose each other. This principle is established through induction and clarifies how we categorize opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the opening statement: the opposite of a single real thing cannot be except one.
This is the core principle.
2
Examine the examples given
The author provides three sets of examples: courage vs. recklessness/cowardice, profligacy vs. apathy, and sharpness vs. dullness. See how each pair of extremes are real opposites, while the middle virtue is not directly opposite to either extreme.
Courage is the middle between recklessness and cowardice.
3
Understand the role of induction
The passage concludes that the principle is established by induction (al-istiqra'). Ask yourself: why is induction necessary? Because we cannot deduce this a priori, we observe it in the world.
Induction means examining many instances to confirm the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضد الواحد إذا كان حقيقياً لا يكون إلا واحداً، فالشجاعة ليس لها ضدان حقيقيان هما التهور والجبن، بل لا تضاد حقيقياً إلا بين الأطراف، كالتهور والجبن، وكالفجور والخمود وكالجربزة والبلادة، كل ذلك الذي ذكرناه من أن الأجناس لا تضاد فيها، وكذا الأنواع إذا لم تكن أنواعاً أخيرة تحت جنس واحد قريب، ومن أن ضد الواحد الحقيقي لا يكون إلا واحداً ثبت بالاستقراء.
And the opposite of a single thing, if it is real, cannot be except one. So courage does not have two real opposites which are recklessness and cowardice. Rather, there is no real opposition except between the extremes, like recklessness and cowardice, and like profligacy and apathy, and like sharpness and dullness. All that which we have mentioned — that genera have no opposition in them, and likewise species if they are not ultimate species under one proximate genus, and that the opposite of a single real thing cannot be except one — is established by induction.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage states a key principle: a single real opposite has only one opposite. It uses the example of courage: courage has two apparent opposites (recklessness and cowardice), but they are not both real opposites of courage. Instead, real opposition exists only between the two extremes themselves (recklessness and cowardice are opposites). Similarly, profligacy and apathy are opposites, and sharpness and dullness are opposites. The passage then reiterates that genera and non-ultimate species are not subject to real opposition. The entire conclusion is based on induction from examining existing things.

Key Terms
ضد الواحد
التضاد الحقيقي
الأطراف
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Courage has two real opposites: recklessness and cowardice.
Correction: In fact, the real opposites are the extremes (recklessness and cowardice), not courage itself. Courage is the middle virtue.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the real opposites of courage?
A) Courage has two real opposites: recklessness and cowardice.
B) Courage has no opposites.
C) The real opposites are recklessness and cowardice, not courage.
D) Only cowardice is the real opposite of courage.

Key Takeaway
A real opposite is always a single extreme, and this is confirmed by observing attributes like courage, profligacy, and sharpness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This passage reveals that real opposition is only between the extremes themselves. Think of a personal struggle: are you caught between two unhealthy extremes, avoiding the virtue entirely? What would it look like to pursue the middle path rather than just avoiding one extreme?
?
The author argues from induction—observing the world—that a single real opposite has only one opposite. In your daily life, do you rely on induction from your experiences to shape your beliefs, or do you jump to conclusions without evidence? This principle calls you to examine the basis of your knowledge. When you hold a belief about right and wrong, have you grounded it in observable reality, or is it merely inherited? Commit to verifying one belief this week by looking at the evidence.
?
Consider the example of courage. How often do you label someone as 'reckless' or 'cowardly' based on a single action, without seeing the virtue of courage that lies between them? This passage reminds you that moral judgment requires discerning the extremes from the mean. The next time you judge another person, pause and ask: are they truly at an extreme, or are they closer to the virtue than you think? And how does your own tendency to extremism blind you to the balanced path? Resolve to practice the virtue of judgment by seeking the middle.

The Three Extreme Pairs and Their Middle Virtues
Sanusi defines three pairs of vices by excess and deficiency and states their middle virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how excess (ifrāṭ) and deficiency (tafrīṭ) define three pairs of vices and their corresponding middle virtues in the context of the Aristotelian doctrine of the mean.

Core Idea
Virtue is the middle between two extremes of excess and deficiency.

In this passage, the author explains the three pairs of vices introduced earlier. For each faculty of the soul—irascible, appetitive, and cognitive—one vice is an excess and the other a deficiency, while the virtue is the balanced middle. This lays the groundwork for the ethical doctrine of the mean, a key concept in both Aristotelian and Islamic ethics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the pattern: for each pair, there is an excess (ifrāṭ), a deficiency (tafrīṭ), and a middle virtue.
The passage uses the structure '... and the middle is ...' for each pair.
2
Identify the three faculties
The passage mentions three faculties: the irascible (quwwa ghaḍabiyya), the appetitive (implied), and the cognitive (quwwa dārika). For each, find the extremes and the middle.
The appetitive faculty is not named explicitly but is understood from the context of desire.
3
Reflect on the middle virtues
Understand that each middle (courage, temperance, wisdom) is the virtuous mean. Ask yourself why these are considered balanced.
This connects to the learning objective about the doctrine of the mean.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالتهور والجبن إلخ ) التهور إفراط طرفي القوة الغضبية والجبن تفريط طرفيها، والمتوسط الشجاعة، والفجور هو غاية ميلان النفس إلى ما تشتهيه والخمود هو غاية سكونها عنه، والمتوسط العفة والجريزة الإفراط في القوة الداركة، والبلادة تفريط فيها، والمتوسطة الحكمة.
Like recklessness and cowardice, etc.: recklessness is the excess of the extremes of the irascible faculty, and cowardice is the deficiency of its extremes, and the middle is courage. Profligacy is the extreme inclination of the soul toward what it desires, and apathy is the extreme cessation from it, and the middle is temperance. Sharpness is excess in the cognitive faculty, and dullness is deficiency in it, and the middle is wisdom.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage defines each of the three pairs of vices introduced earlier. For each pair, one vice is an excess (ifrat) and the other a deficiency (tafrit) relative to a specific faculty. The first pair relates to the irascible faculty (quwwa ghadabiyya): recklessness is excess, cowardice is deficiency, and the middle virtue is courage. The second pair relates to the appetitive faculty: profligacy (fujur) is excess, apathy (khumud) is deficiency, and the middle is temperance (‘iffa). The third pair relates to the cognitive faculty: sharpness (jiraza) is excess, dullness (balada) is deficiency, and the middle is wisdom (hikma). This illustrates the Aristotelian doctrine of the mean.

Key Terms

القوة الداركة

الفجور
الخمود
الجريزة
البلادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both extremes are equally bad but the middle is just average or mediocre.
Correction: The extremes are defined as excess and deficiency relative to a faculty; the middle is the virtuous ideal, not mediocrity. Each middle is a positive character trait.

Checkpoint
What is the middle virtue between recklessness (تهور) and cowardice (جبن)?
A) Temperance
B) Wisdom
C) Courage
D) Profligacy

Key Takeaway
Virtue is the middle path between excess and deficiency in each faculty of the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Consider your use of intellect: are you prone to overthinking and cunning (sharpness) or laziness in thought (dullness)? What step will you take this week to seek wisdom, the balanced middle, in your decisions?

Objection to Induction and Its Response
The author presents and answers the first objection against the inductive proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the inductive proof regarding opposition and how the response resolves the apparent circularity by invoking the criterion of extreme difference.

Core Idea
Induction's circularity is resolved by the criterion of extreme difference.

In this passage, the author considers an objection to the inductive proof that real opposition only exists between two species of the same genus. The objection claims the argument is circular because it assumes the very classification it tries to prove. The response provides a clear criterion—the absence of extreme difference—to break the cycle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the logical structure of the objection: it claims a circular reasoning.
Notice the example of profligacy and temperance.
2
Examine the example
Understand why profligacy and temperance are considered species of two genera, and why this poses a problem for the inductive claim.
3
Analyze the response
Notice that the response does not deny the circularity but provides a way out: the criterion of 'ghayat al-khilaf' (extreme difference). Ask yourself how this criterion resolves the circle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن اعترض على إثباته بالاستقراء أيضاً بوجوه الأول أن معنى الاستقراء في انحصار التضاد بين نوعين من جنس هو أنا وجدناه فيما بينهما دون غيرهما، ولا طريق إلى نفيه عن الفجور والعفة مثلا، سوى أنه لا يكون إلا فيما بين نوعين من جنس واحد وهذان نوعان من جنسين، وفيه دور ظاهر. والجواب : أن الطريق إلى ذلك انتفاء غاية الخلاف بينهما.
But an objection was raised against establishing it by induction also in several ways. The first: that the meaning of induction in the restriction of opposition to two species of a genus is that we found it between them and not between others. There is no way to deny it concerning profligacy and temperance, for example, except that it occurs only between two species of one genus, but these two are species of two genera, and this contains an apparent circle. The answer: that the way to that is the non-existence of extreme difference between them.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the first of several objections to using induction to prove that real opposition only exists between two species of the same genus. The objection argues that induction assumes the very conclusion it seeks to prove: to say that we have found opposition only within a genus presupposes that we know what species are under one genus. For example, profligacy and temperance appear to be under different genera, but according to the principle they would have to be under one genus for opposition to exist. This is circular. The answer is that the criterion for denying opposition between them is the lack of extreme difference (ghayat al-khilaf). That is, if two things are not at opposite extremes, they cannot be real opposites, regardless of their genus.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
دور
غاية الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Induction is invalid because it is circular.
Correction: The circularity is resolved by applying the criterion of extreme difference, which does not rely on prior classification.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why is the inductive proof considered circular regarding profligacy and temperance?
A) They are not opposites at all.
B) They are species of two genera, but the rule only applies within one genus.
C) The induction was not thorough.
D) The answer does not address the objection.

Key Takeaway
The response to the circularity objection shows that the criterion for determining real opposition is the presence of extreme difference, not prior genus classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Virtue and Vice: Privation and Accidental Opposition
Commentary on virtue and vice as privative and accidental opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways in which virtue and vice are opposites: as privation and as accidental opposition.

Core Idea
Virtue and vice are not true genera but privative and accidental opposites.

In this lesson, we explore the nature of the opposition between virtue and vice. The author presents two key answers to a potential doubt, showing that the opposition is not between genera but between accidental attributes. This clarifies how good/evil and virtue/vice relate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation to get the general meaning.
Notice the author's use of 'or' (أو) between the two answers.
2
Identify the two answers
The commentator provides two answers to a doubt. Find them: the first about privation and possession, the second about accidental opposition.
3
Understand privation
Privation means that one thing is the absence of another. Here vice is the absence of virtue, just as evil is the absence of good.
This is a type of opposition (تقابل العدم والملكة).
4
Understand accidental opposition
The second answer says the opposition is accidental (بالعرض). That means it applies to the accidental concepts (goodness, evilness, virtue, vice) not to underlying genera.
Thus two opposites can fall under one genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الحادي عشر : المتقابلان أمران لا يجتمعان
احب الكتاب أن الفضيلة والرذيلة أيضاً جنسان بينهما تضاد كالخير والشر، ثم أشار إلى الجواب أولاً بأن الكل من قبيل العدم والملكة فإن الرذيلة عدم الفضيلة كما أن الشرية عدم الخيرية. وثانياً بأن التضاد في الكل بالعرض ؛ أي هذه الأمور الأربعة أمور عارضة ليس شيء منها جنساً لما تحته على قياس ما عرفت، فكون الشيء خيراً ضد لكونه شراً، كما أن كونه فضيلة ضد لكونه رذيلة، فلم يثبت تضاد بين الأجناس بل بين العوارض التي يجوز أن يكون كل متضادين منها تحت جنس واحد . قوله : أن الفضيلة والرذيلة إلخ ) فالمراد منهما مفهوماهما كما في الخير والشر، وهو الظاهر المتبادر ويكون النقضان واردين على القاعدة الأولى . قوله: (أشار إلى الجواب أولاً إلخ (فالجوابان من شبهة واحدة منشؤها صورتان، فكل واحد من الجوابين جواب عن كلا النقيضين فكان الظاهر الواو ، وإنما أورد كلمة . . ( أو ) نظراً إلى عموم قوله : و ما يتوهم يعني ما يتوهم بخلاف ذلك لا يخلو عن هذين الأمرين. قوله : (بل بين العوارض التي يجوز إلخ ) إشارة إلى أن جواز دخولهما تحت جنس واحد كاف لنا، وأن الناقض للقاعدة الثانية يلزمه إثبات عدم الدخول . قوله : (وثانياً بأن التضاد في الكل بالعرض أي في العرض كما في جلست بالمسجد، فعلى هذا تطبيق الجواب ظاهر.
The Fourth Observatory - The Eleventh Goal: The two opposites are two things that do not coexist. The author [said]: that virtue and vice are also two genera between which there is opposition, like good and evil. Then he indicated the answer first: that both are of the type of privation and possession, for vice is the absence of virtue just as evilness is the absence of goodness. And second: that the opposition in all is accidental; i.e., these four things are accidental matters, none of them being a genus for what is under it on the analogy of what you have learned. So a thing's being good is opposite to its being evil, just as its being virtue is opposite to its being vice. Thus opposition between genera is not established, but rather between accidents which it is possible for each of two opposites to be under one genus. His saying: 'that virtue and vice etc.' So what is meant by them is their concepts as in good and evil, and that is the apparent and immediate understanding, and the two objections are directed at the first principle. His saying: 'he indicated the answer first etc.' So the two answers arise from one doubt whose source is two forms, so each of the two answers is an answer to both of the two opposites, so the apparent would be the waw, but he only brought the word 'aw' due to the generality of his saying: 'and what is imagined' meaning what is imagined contrary to that is not free from these two matters. His saying: 'but between the accidents which it is possible etc.' is an indication that the permissibility of their being under one genus is sufficient for us, and that one who objects to the second principle must prove non-entry. His saying: 'and second that the opposition in all is accidental', i.e., in the accident like your sitting in the mosque, so on this the application of the answer is apparent.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of how virtue and vice are opposites. The author begins by stating that virtue and vice are two genera that have opposition, similar to good and evil. He then provides two answers to a potential doubt. The first answer is that the opposition is of the type of privation and possession: vice is the absence of virtue, just as evilness is the absence of goodness. The second answer is that the opposition is accidental; these four things (good, evil, virtue, vice) are accidental attributes, not genera. So a thing's being good is opposite to its being evil, but there is no real opposition between the genera; rather, the opposition is between the accidents, which can both be under the same genus. The commentary explains that the words 'virtue' and 'vice' refer to their concepts, which are apparent. The two answers respond to two forms of a single doubt. The author uses 'or' instead of 'and' because the doubt is general. The commentary further clarifies that the permissibility of the two accidents being under one genus is sufficient to refute the objection, and that the accidental opposition is like the accident of sitting in a mosque.

A Real Opposite Has Only One Opposite
The author proves by induction that a single real opposite is unique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that a real opposite is unique and how it applies to virtues and their extremes.

Core Idea
A real opposite is always singular—nothing has two real opposites.

This passage establishes a key logical principle: if something has a real opposite, it has exactly one. The author uses examples from moral psychology to illustrate this, showing that virtues like courage are means between two extremes, and the real opposition is between the extremes themselves, not between the virtue and an extreme.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the claim.
Notice the style: a concise statement followed by examples and a proof method.
2
Identify the principle
Locate the statement: 'the opposite of one, if real, is only one.' This is the core claim.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the three examples: courage (rashness/cowardice), chastity (licentiousness/insensibility), wisdom (sharpness/dullness). Understand that the extremes are opposites of each other, not of the mean.
The commentary defines each term precisely.
4
Understand the proof method
The author says the principle is established by induction (استقراء) and examination of existents. Reflect on why this is convincing.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common mistake that courage has two opposites. The passage shows why this is not the case.
This connects to the checkpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضد الواحد إذا كان حقيقياً لا يكون إلا واحداً، فالشجاعة ليس لها ضدان هما التهور والجبن، بل لا تضاد حقيقياً إلا بين الأطراف، كالتهور والجبن، وكالفجور والخمود وكالجربزة والبلادة ) كل ذلك ( الذي ذكرناه من أن الأجناس لا تضاد فيها، وكذا الأنواع إذا لم تكن أنواعاً أخيرة تحت جنس واحد قريب، ومن أن ضد الواحد الحقيقي لا يكون إلا واحداً ثبت بالاستقراء)، وتتبع أحوال الموجودات،
قوله : ( فالشجاعة إلخ) أي على تقدير كونهما ضداً حقيقياً.
قوله : (كالتهور والجبن إلخ ) التهور إفراط طرفي القوة الغضبية والجبن تفريط طرفيها، والمتوسط الشجاعة، والفجور هو غاية ميلان النفس إلى ما تشتهيه والخمود هو غاية سكونها عنه، والمتوسط العفة والجريزة الإفراط في القوة الداركة، والبلادة تفريط فيها، والمتوسطة الحكمة .
And the opposite of one, if it is real, is only one. So courage does not have two opposites, which are rashness and cowardice. Rather, real opposition is only between the extremes, like rashness and cowardice, and like licentiousness and insensibility, and like sharpness and dullness. All that which we have mentioned: that genera have no opposition in them, and likewise species if they are not final species under one proximate genus, and that the real opposite of one is only one – is established by induction and by tracking the states of existents. His saying: 'so courage etc.' meaning on the assumption that they are real opposites. His saying: 'like rashness and cowardice etc.' Rashness is excess of the two extremes of the irascible faculty, and cowardice is deficiency of its two extremes, and the mean is courage. Licentiousness is the utmost inclination of the soul towards what it desires, and insensibility is the utmost stillness from it, and the mean is chastity. Sharpness is excess in the cognitive faculty, and dullness is deficiency in it, and the mean is wisdom.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a proof and classification. It states the principle that a real opposite is unique: if something has a real opposite, it has only one. The example is courage: it does not have two real opposites (rashness and cowardice) because real opposition is only between extremes. The passage then lists examples: rashness and cowardice (opposites of each other, not of courage), licentiousness and insensibility, and sharpness and dullness. The author notes that this principle (that genera have no opposition, species under a proximate genus have no opposition, and a real opposite is unique) is established by induction and examination of existents. The commentary explains that the statement about courage is on the assumption that rashness and cowardice are real opposites, which they are not. It then defines the extremes for each virtue: rashness is excess of the irascible faculty, cowardice is deficiency, courage is the mean; licentiousness is excess of desire, insensibility is deficiency, chastity is the mean; sharpness is excess of the cognitive faculty, dullness is deficiency, wisdom is the mean.

تضاد
أطراف
استقراء
شجاعة

Misconception: Courage has two opposites: rashness and cowardice.
Correction: Real opposition is only between extremes; rashness and cowardice are opposites of each other, not of courage.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A real opposite is unique, and real opposition occurs only between extremes of a virtue, not between the virtue and an extreme.

The Specificity of al-Ḍiddān Among Philosophers and Theologians
Defines opposites and contrasts philosophers' vs theologians' use of 'contraries'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' definition of 'al-ḍiddān' (two contraries) is more specific than the theologians' definition, and why correlatives and some similars are excluded.

Core Idea
The philosophers' 'contraries' are narrower because they require utmost opposition.

This passage from al-Ījī's Mawāqif introduces the concept of opposition and clarifies a key terminological difference between philosophers and theologians. The term 'al-ḍiddān' (two contraries) is used differently by each group, with philosophers applying a stricter condition of utmost difference. Understanding this distinction is crucial for following later arguments about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-mutaqābilān
Read the Arabic sentence defining al-mutaqābilān as two things that cannot coexist without decisive proof. This sets the foundation.
2
Compare the scope of al-ḍiddān
Notice that the philosophers' al-ḍiddān is said to be 'more specific' than the theologians'. Look for the reason given in the next sentences.
3
Examine the examples: correlatives and similars
The passage uses al-mutaḍāyifān (correlatives) and al-mutamāthilān (similars) to show how theologians include them but philosophers exclude them. Understand the condition of 'utmost difference' (ghāyat al-khilāf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الحادي عشر : المتقابلان أمران لا يجتمعان دون البرهان القطعي . ( والضدان عندهم أخص مما عند المتكلمين)؛ لأن المتضائفين على تقدير وجودهما داخلان في الضدين على مقتضى تعريفهم، دون تعريف الحكماء. قيل : وكذا الحال في المتماثلين. قوله : ( على تقدير وجودهما) يعني أن المتضائفين قد اختلف في وجودهما، فعلى القول بوجودهما يكونان داخلين في الضدين على مقتضى تعريف المتكلمين دون تعريف الحكماء، وليس المراد أنهما على فرض وجودهما كذلك حتى يرد أن مادة الافتراق يجب أن تكون متحققة حتى يحصل الجزم بالأخصية، ولأن المتكلمين قائلون بدخولهما في تعريف الضدين. قوله : ( وكذا الحال في المتماثلين أي في بعض المتماثلين على القول بامتناع اجتماعهما، فإنهما داخلان في تعريف الضدين للمتكلمين خارجان عن تعريفهما للحكماء؛ لاعتبار غاية الخلاف فيه. وهذا لا ينافي ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره سابقاً من عدم دخولهما في تعريف المتكلمين؛ لأن المراد منه بجميع أفرادهما قطعا؛ لأن المتوهم جعله دليلا على وجوب جعلهما قسماً من المتضادين.
Observation 4 - Aim 11: The two opposites are two things that do not come together without decisive proof. (And the two contraries (al-ḍiddān) according to them are more specific than according to the theologians); because the two correlatives (al-mutaḍāyifān), on the assumption of their existence, are included in the two contraries according to the requirement of their definition, but not according to the definition of the philosophers. It was said: Likewise is the case with the two similars (al-mutamāthilān). His statement: (on the assumption of their existence) means that the two correlatives have been differed regarding their existence. So on the view of their existence, they enter into the two contraries according to the requirement of the theologians' definition, not the philosophers' definition. It is not intended that they are so on the assumption of their existence such that it would be objected that the point of separation must be realized in order to be certain of the specificity, and because the theologians hold that they enter into the definition of contraries. His statement: (Likewise is the case with the two similars) i.e., in some of the similars on the view of the impossibility of their coexistence, for they enter into the definition of the two contraries for the theologians and are excluded from their definition for the philosophers due to considering the utmost difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) in it. And this does not contradict what the commentator (quddisa sirruhu) mentioned earlier of their non-inclusion in the theologians' definition; because what is meant by it is all their individuals definitely; because the objector made it a proof for the necessity of making them a division of the two contraries.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of opposition (al-mutaqābilān) and clarifies the scope of the term 'al-ḍiddān' (two contraries) according to the philosophers versus the theologians. The author first states the general rule: the two opposites cannot coexist without decisive proof. Then he explains that the philosophers' definition of 'al-ḍiddān' is more specific than the theologians' because the philosophers exclude correlatives (al-mutaḍāyifān) and some similars (al-mutamāthilān). The commentator elaborates: if correlatives exist, theologians include them in their definition, but philosophers do not. Similarly, among similars, only those that cannot coexist are included by theologians but excluded by philosophers because philosophers require 'utmost difference' (ghāyat al-khilāf). The commentator reassures that this does not contradict earlier statements, as the objector intended the general category.

Key Terms
المتقابلان
الضدان
المتضائفان
المتماثلان
غاية الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers' definition of al-ḍiddān is broader than the theologians'.
Correction: Actually, the philosophers' definition is narrower because it excludes correlatives and some similars, requiring utmost difference.

Checkpoint

B) Because philosophers exclude correlatives and some similars due to the requirement of utmost difference
C) Because theologians exclude all similars
D) Because philosophers require empirical proof for coexistence

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' definition of contraries is narrower because it requires utmost difference, excluding correlatives and some similars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about 'opposites' in your own life—like good and evil, truth and falsehood—do you treat them as absolute contraries that cannot coexist, or do you recognize that they may be interrelated? The philosophers' condition of 'utmost difference' challenges you to examine whether your oppositions are real or merely assumed. Are there areas where you have created false dichotomies that limit your understanding? Commit to re-examining one such opposition this week.
?
The passage shows that even scholars have differing definitions of the same term. In your religious study, do you assume that terms have universally agreed-upon meanings? This lesson reminds you to pay attention to how scholars define their terms. How can you cultivate the intellectual humility to ask 'What do you mean by that?' before disagreeing? Pledge to check definitions before debating an idea.
?
The theologians included correlatives as contraries; the philosophers excluded them because correlatives are interdependent. In your relationships, do you see people as opponents (contraries) or as mutually dependent (correlatives)? Could a conflict in your life be better understood as a correlation where each side needs the other? Take time to identify one such relationship and consider how seeing it as correlative might change your approach.

Classification of Privation and Possession into Well-Known and Real
Classifies privation-possession into two types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between well-known and real privation and possession, and how the subject's capacity determines the type.

Core Idea
Privation requires a subject naturally capable of the attribute, otherwise it is not true privation.

This lesson explores a key division within the second type of opposition, where one opposite is the negation of the other. The author distinguishes between well-known privation-possession, which considers the subject's capacity at a specific time, and real privation-possession, which considers the subject's nature by species or genus. This distinction is crucial for precise logical and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the division and the examples given.
The passage uses technical terms like 'kawsaj' (bearded youth) and 'amrad' (beardless youth).
2
Identify the two types of privation-possession
Look for the two categories: 'well-known' (mashhūriyān) and 'real' (ḥaqīqiyān). Notice how each is defined by the capacity of the subject.
Well-known considers capacity at a particular time; real considers capacity by species or genus.
3
Examine the examples for each type
For well-known: the bearded youth lacking a beard at a time he should have it. For real: blindness for a mole, lack of beard for a woman, blindness for a scorpion, rest for a mountain. Understand why each fits its category.
The example of the separate intellect (mufāriq) is given as a case where real privation does not apply because it is not capable.
4
Understand the concluding condition for real privation
The passage states that mere impossibility of coexistence is not enough; the relation to the subject must be part of the concept.
This ensures we do not mistakenly treat any absence as privation.
5
Apply the distinction to a new example
Think of another pair that could be well-known or real privation, and explain why.
For instance, 'baldness' for a man vs. a rock.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني) وهو أن يكون أحد المتقابلين سلباً للآخر ينقسم أيضاً إلى قسمين؛ لأنه ( إن اعتبر فيه نسبتهما إلى قابل للأمر الوجودي، فعدم وملكة فإن اعتبر قبوله له أي قبول ذلك القابل للأمر الوجودي في ذلك الوقت كالكوسج فإنه يعني كونه كوسجا عدم اللحية عمن من شأنه في الوقت أن يكون ملتحياً لا للأمرد أي يقال الكوسج لمن ذكر لا للأمرد الذي ليس من شأنه اللحية في ذلك الوقت فهو العدم والملكة المشهوريان. وإن اعتبر قبوله له أعم من ذلك، بل بحسب نوعه كالعمى للأكمه، وعدم اللحية للمرأة، أو جنسه القريب أو البعيد، فالأول كالعمى للعقرب فإن البصر من شأن جنسها القريب أعني الحيوان، والثاني كالسكون المقابل للحركة الإرادية للجبل، فإن جنسه البعيد أعني الجسم الذي هو فوق الجماد قابل للحركة الإرادية، لا كعدم القيام بالغير للمفارق؛ إذ ليس من شأن المفارق القيام بالغير، ولا من شأن نوعه أو جنسه مطلقاً إذ لم يجعل الجوهر جنساً له، فهو العدم والملكة الحقيقيان)، فالحقيقي من العدم امتناع الاجتماع بالنسبة إلى الموضوع القابل لا يكفي في العدم والملكة، بل لا بد مع ذلك أن تكون النسبة إليه مأخوذة في مفهوم العدمي.
And the second (type) is that one of the two opposites is the negation of the other; it is also divided into two parts: because if their relation to a subject capable of existential attribute is considered, then it is privation and possession. For if its capacity is considered—i.e., the capacity of that subject for the existential attribute in that time—like the bearded youth (al-kawsaj), for he is one whose beard is lacking from someone whose character it is to have a beard at that time, not from the beardless youth (al-amrad), i.e., it is said 'bearded youth' to the one mentioned, not to the beardless youth who does not have the character of having a beard at that time—then it is the well-known privation and possession (al-ʿadam wa al-milkatu al-mashhūriyān). And if his capacity is considered more general than that, but rather according to his species, like blindness for the mole, and lack of beard for a woman, or his near or distant genus—then the first is like blindness for the scorpion, for sight is of the character of its near genus, namely the animal; and the second is like rest (sukūn) corresponding to voluntary motion for the mountain, for its distant genus, namely the body which is above inanimate, is capable of voluntary motion—unlike the lack of subsistence in another (qiyām bi al-ghayr) for the separate intellect (al-mufāriq), for it is not of the character of the separate intellect to subsist in another, nor of its species or its genus at all, since the substance (jawhar) is not made a genus for it—then it is the real privation and possession (al-ʿadam wa al-milkatu al-ḥaqīqiyān). So the real of privation is [such that] the impossibility of coexistence with respect to the receptive subject is not sufficient in privation and possession; rather, along with that, the relation to it must be taken into consideration in the concept of the privative term.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage classifies the second type of opposition, namely when one opposite is the negation of the other (salb wa ījāb). It divides this into two categories based on how the privation is considered in relation to a subject capable of possessing the attribute. The first type considers the subject's capacity at a specific time; it is called 'well-known privation and possession' (al-ʿadam wa al-milkatu al-mashhūriyān). The example given is the bearded youth (al-kawsaj), who lacks a beard at a time when it is his natural disposition to have one, as opposed to a beardless youth (al-amrad) who is not yet capable. The second type considers capacity more broadly—by species (e.g., blindness for a mole, which lacks sight by its species), by near genus (e.g., lack of sight for a scorpion, whose near genus 'animal' is capable of sight), or by distant genus (e.g., rest for a mountain, whose distant genus 'body' is capable of voluntary motion). This is real privation and possession (al-ḥaqīqiyān). The passage concludes with an important nuance: mere impossibility of coexistence with a receptive subject is not enough for real privation; the relation to the subject must be part of the concept of the privative term.

Key Terms

العدم والملكة
المشهوريان
الحقيقيان
الكوسج
الأمرد
الأكمه
المفارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any lack of an attribute in a subject qualifies as privation.
Correction: Privation requires the subject to be naturally capable of the attribute; otherwise it is mere negation.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of well-known privation-possession (al-mashhūriyān)?
A) Blindness for a mole
B) Lack of beard for a woman
C) A bearded youth lacking a beard when he should have one
D) Rest for a mountain

Key Takeaway
Privation is not merely absence; it requires that the subject is naturally disposed to possess the attribute, either at a specific time (well-known) or by its nature (real).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a quality you believe you lack, such as patience in a trial. According to this lesson, true privation requires that you be naturally capable of that quality at that time or by your nature. Have you ever considered that your lack might be a 'real privation' because you are simply not capable of it at this moment? How does that change your self-reproach and your reliance on Allah's grace?
?
The separate intellect is not blamed for lacking subsistence in another because it is not its nature. In your own life, where are you blaming yourself for lacking something that is not actually part of your created capacity? What would it look like to accept Allah's wisdom in your design and focus on the attributes He has given you?
?
Real privation considers the subject's nature. When you feel deficient, do you first ask whether that deficiency is even possible for you? How often do you impose expectations from others or own assumptions that do not align with your true nature? Can you commit to examining one area where you feel lacking and ask, 'Is this truly a real privation for me?' before judging yourself harshly?

The Condition for Correlatives: A Single Subject
Defines the condition of a single subject for correlatives and addresses privations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the necessary condition for two things to be considered correlatives (متقابلان) and how the presence or absence of an intermediate affects the judgment of their coexistence in a single subject.

Core Idea
Two things are correlatives only if they are relative to one subject.

This passage from al-Mawaqif explains a foundational concept in Islamic logic and theology: what makes two things truly opposite? The author, al-Iji, clarifies that mere opposition in meaning is not enough; the two must be considered in relation to a single subject. This condition is especially important when dealing with privations (عدميان), as the existence of an intermediate can resolve apparent difficulties. As you read, pay attention to how the condition of a single subject (محل واحد) is applied.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Note the key terms like 'محل واحد' and 'وساطة'. Try to identify the main claim and the examples.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Understand the condition
The condition is that two correlatives must be related to a single subject. Why is this necessary? Because the mind judges impossibility of coexistence only when they are relative to the same thing.
3
Examine the exception for privations
When there is no intermediate between two privations, they are not correlatives because they are not relative to a single subject. But if there is an intermediate, the objection is refuted.
The example of non-possibility and non-thingness illustrates this.
4
Apply the concept
Think of other pairs of opposites and check if they satisfy the condition of a single subject. This reinforces the logical criterion.
This step connects theory to practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لاجتماعهما في كل موجود إلخ) يعني بد في المتقابلين من نسبتهما إلى محل واحد، حتى يحكم العقل بامتناع اجتماعهما فيه فإن لم يكن بين ملكتي العدمين المضافين واسطة أصلاً بأن يكون كل منهما من الأمور الشاملة كالشيء والممكن العام، أو كلاهما شامل لجميع الموجودات كالقيام بالنفس والقيام بالغير، فلا تقابل بين عدميهما لانتفاء نسبتهما إلى محل واحد وإن كان بينهما واسطة يجتمع العدمان فيه فاندفع الإيراد عليه باللاممكنية واللاشيئية وبعدم القيام بالنفس وعدم القيام بالغير فإنهما عدميان لا يجتمعان في موجود مغاير لما أضيف إليه لعدم الواسطة بين ما أضيفا إليه .
He said: (Regarding the statement: 'for their coexistence in every existent, etc.') meaning that it is necessary for the two correlatives that they be related to a single subject, so that the mind judges the impossibility of their coexistence in it. If there is no intermediate between the two added privations at all, such that each of them is among universal matters like 'thing' and 'general possible', or both encompass all existents like self-subsistence and subsistence by another, then there is no correlation between their privations due to the lack of their relation to a single subject. But if there is an intermediate between them in which the two privations coexist, then the objection regarding 'non-possibility' and 'non-thingness' and 'non-self-subsistence' and 'non-subsistence by another' is refuted, for they are two privations that do not coexist in an existent other than that to which they are added, due to the lack of an intermediate between that to which they are added.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for two things to be considered correlatives (متقابلان). The author explains that for two things to be truly opposite, they must be considered in relation to a single subject (محل واحد) so that the mind can judge that they cannot coexist in that subject. If there is no intermediate between two privations (عدمان مضافان) and they are both universal (like 'thing' and 'general possible') or both encompass all existents (like self-subsistence and subsistence by another), then there is no correlation because they are not relative to a single subject. However, if there is an intermediate in which both privations can coexist, then the objection raised against pairs like non-possibility and non-thingness is refuted. The key point is that the correlation depends on the relationship to a common subject.

Key Terms
المتقابلان
محل واحد
عدميان
وساطة
اللاممكنية
اللاشيئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two privations are automatically correlatives.
Correction: They must be relative to a single subject, and the presence of an intermediate is necessary for them to be considered correlatives.

Checkpoint
What is necessary for two privations to be considered correlatives (متقابلان)?
A) They must be opposite in meaning
B) They must be related to a single subject and have an intermediate in which they can coexist
C) They must be universal in scope
D) They must be explicitly mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Two things are correlatives only if they are related to a single subject, and for privations, an intermediate must exist for them to be considered opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reflect on a time when you assumed two internal states (e.g., hope and fear of Allah) were opposites that couldn't coexist. The passage teaches that true opposition requires a single subject. Where in your life have you judged something as contradictory to your faith (e.g., seeking worldly success vs. piety) without examining whether they share a common subject? This week, identify one such conflict and deliberately relate both aspects to the same subject (e.g., pleasing Allah) so you can judge their compatibility. What practical step will you take to integrate them?

The example of privations shows that without an intermediate, no true correlation exists. Is there a relationship or disagreement in your life where you feel trapped in opposition because you haven't identified the intermediate that could allow coexistence? For instance, between your desire for personal freedom and your duties to family. Between these two, what intermediate (e.g., 'service to Allah') could be the subject that unites them? Two Objections on Privations and Their Answers
Two objections and answers about pairs of privations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defend the definition of correlation against objections involving pairs of privations that seem to meet the criteria but fail due to impossibility of an intermediate or lack of intrinsic relation.

Core Idea
Genuine correlation requires an intrinsic relation, not just co-occurrence in thought.

In this passage, the commentator addresses two objections that challenge the standard definition of correlation (تقابل). The first objection concerns the pair of non-possibility and non-thingness, while the second deals with absence of squint and absence of sight. Both objections are answered by showing why these pairs do not satisfy the conditions for genuine correlation. Let's examine each response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first objection and answer
Read the first objection about non-possibility and non-thingness. Note the commentator's claim that if one is found in a concept, the other is also found. Then see how the answer refutes this by showing the impossibility of a concept that includes both.
2
Understand the second objection and answer
Read the second objection about absence of squint and absence of sight both being negated of a wall. The answer explains that the correlation is not intrinsic but due to implication, so they fall outside the definition.
3
Reflect on criteria for genuine correlation
Consider what makes a correlation genuine: intrinsic relation (تقابل بالذات) and the possibility of an intermediate. Contrast with the pairs discussed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما قاله الشارح قدس سره في حواشي التجريد بأنه يكفي في نفي التقابل بين اللاممكنية واللاشيئية كونهما بحيث لو وجد أحدهما في مفهوم وجد الآخر فيه، ففيه أن فرض وجود مفهوم بينهما محال، فيجوز أن يستلزم المحال، أعني امتناع الاجتماع. وأما إيراد شارح التجريد من أن عدم الحول عما من شأن شخصه أن يكون أحول وعدم قابلية البصر كلاهما مسلوبان عن الجدار فلا يصح قوله : لاجتماعهما في كل موجود مغاير لما أضيف فجوابه: أن التقابل بينهما ليس بالذات بل باعتبار استلزام الحول وجود البصر، فهما خارجان عن تعريف المتقابلين .
As for what the commentator (may God sanctify his secret) said in the glosses on al-Tajrid, that it suffices for negating the correlation between non-possibility and non-thingness that they are such that if one of them is found in a concept, the other is found in it, the answer is that supposing a concept between them is impossible, so it may entail the impossible, i.e., the impossibility of coexistence. And as for the objection of the commentator on al-Tajrid that absence of squint from one whose nature is to be squint-eyed and absence of the capacity for sight are both negated of the wall, so his statement 'for their coexistence in every existent other than that to which they are added' is not valid, the answer is that the correlation between them is not intrinsic but rather by virtue of the implication of squint for the existence of sight, so they are outside the definition of the two correlatives.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents two objections and their answers. The first objection from the commentator on al-Tajrid claims that for negating correlation between 'non-possibility' and 'non-thingness', it suffices that if one is found in a concept, the other is also found. The answer is that it is impossible to suppose a concept between them, so this may entail the impossible (i.e., the impossibility of coexistence), which is not a valid correlation. The second objection raises the case of 'absence of squint' from one whose nature is to be squint-eyed and 'absence of the capacity for sight', both negated of the wall. The objection is that they would then coexist in the wall, but the answer is that the correlation is not intrinsic but due to the implication of squint for the existence of sight. Therefore, these pairs are outside the definition of correlatives.

Key Terms
اللاممكنية
اللاشيئية
عدم الحول
عدم القابلية
تقابل بالذات
استلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absence of an intermediate or co-occurrence in a concept is enough to establish correlation between two privations.
Correction: For genuine correlation, the relation must be intrinsic (بالذات); mere co-privation or co-negation does not suffice, as shown by the answers.

Checkpoint

B) Because the correlation is not intrinsic but due to implication
C) Because they are both privations
D) Because they have no intermediate

Key Takeaway
Genuine correlation between privations must be intrinsic; otherwise, the pair does not qualify as true correlatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Opposition of Blindness and Non-Blindness
Analyzes the opposition between blindness and non-blindness as سلب وإيجاب.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalam analysis of 'blindness' and 'non-blindness' shows that their opposition is fundamentally between existence and non-existence, not between two positive concepts.

Core Idea
Conceptual difference does not change existential opposition.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle semantic and logical issue: how can 'non-blindness' be opposed to 'blindness' when it seems to be a broader concept? He argues that the opposition is not between two positive properties but between an existent (sight) and its negation. This clarifies the classification of such terms under the category of سلب وإيجاب and addresses a critique from the commentator on al-Tajrid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation to understand the argument.
The passage is dense but foundational.
2
Understand the claim about اللاعمى
The author says اللاعمى is a general concept (مفهوم عام) that cannot be directly attributed to a subject. Therefore it is not the direct opposite of العمى.
This is the key insight.
3
Identify the two interpretations
The author offers two ways to understand the opposition: either as absence of sight or absence of capacity. In both cases, the opposition is between an existent (sight or capacity) and its absence.
Both lead to the same conclusion.
4
Trace the response to the objection
The commentator on al-Tajrid argued that اللاعمى and البصر differ in concept, so they cannot be the same in truth. The author replies that they are the same in terms of truth (صدق) even if concepts differ, so the opposition is still existent vs non-existent.
This resolves the apparent paradox.
5
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Consider how this analysis fits into the larger kalam framework of rational judgments (السلب والإيجاب). Why does it matter that the opposition is between existence and non-existence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما العمى فهو انتفاء إلخ ) يعني أن اللاعمى مفهوم عام، لا يمكن اتصاف المحل به من حيث عمومه، فلا يكون من حيث هو مقابلاً للعمى، بل إما في ضمن انتفاء البصر، أو انتفاء القابلية. وعلى التقديرين التقابل بين الوجودي والعدمي، فلا نقض وقس على ذلك الجواب عن جميع صور العدميين إذا كان أحدهما سلباً للآخر. قوله: (فهو البصر بعينه أي من حيث الصدق، وإن تغايرا في المفهوم، فالتقابل بينهما في الحقيقة تقابل بين الوجودي والعدمي. وبهذا اندفع ما أورده الشارح قدس سره في حواشي التجريد من أن التغاير بينهما في المفهوم لا شبهة فيه، وإن كانا متلازمين في الوجود .
He said: (As for blindness, it is the absence, etc.) meaning that non-blindness is a general concept, and the subject cannot be described by it in terms of its generality, so it is not, as such, an opposite of blindness, but rather either within the absence of sight or the absence of capacity. In both cases, the opposition is between the existent and the non-existent, so there is no contradiction. Apply that to the answer for all forms of privations when one of them is the negation of the other. He said: (So it is sight itself) i.e., in terms of truth, even if they differ in concept, so the opposition between them in reality is opposition between the existent and the non-existent. By this, what the commentator (may God sanctify his secret) raised in the glosses on al-Tajrid is repelled, that the difference between them in concept is beyond doubt, even if they are coexistent in existence.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage interprets the pair 'blindness' (العمى) and 'non-blindness' (اللاعمى). It explains that 'non-blindness' is a general concept that cannot be directly attributed to a subject in its generality, so it is not an opposite of blindness per se. Instead, it is either included in 'absence of sight' or 'absence of capacity'. In both cases, the opposition is between an existent (such as sight) and a non-existent (the absence), thus it falls under the category of existence and non-existence. The author then responds to an objection from the commentator on al-Tajrid: although 'non-blindness' and 'sight' differ in concept, they are the same in terms of truth (صدق), so the opposition is really between existent and non-existent. This objection attempted to show a difference in concept, but the answer is that the correlation is based on truth, not concept.

Key Terms
العمى
اللاعمى
انتفاء البصر
انتفاء القابلية
الوجودي
العدمي
السلب والإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-blindness and sight are opposites based purely on concept.
Correction: Their opposition is actually based on truth (صدق), i.e., existence vs non-existence, not just conceptual difference.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the true nature of the opposition between العمى and اللاعمى?
A) It is a case of العدم والملكة
B) It is a case of السلب والإيجاب between the existent and the non-existent
C) It is a case of التضاد
D) It is not a genuine opposition

Key Takeaway
The opposition between العمى and اللاعمى is ultimately between existence and non-existence, not between two positive concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever insisted on a conceptual difference between two things—like 'blindness' and 'non-blindness'—only to realize that in reality they are one and the same? How does that change how you view your own stubbornness in arguments?
?
This lesson teaches that a general concept like 'non-blindness' cannot describe a subject directly. In your life, do you sometimes label yourself or others with vague, general categories that prevent you from seeing the specific reality of their actions or intentions? Ask Allah to grant you precision in judgment.
?
The commentator objected based on conceptual difference, but the author upheld the existential truth. When you face a conflict between what you think and what is actually true, are you willing to let go of your conceptual preconceptions for the sake of truth? Set a specific intention to practice this humility today.

Conceptual Difference Despite Coexistence: The Case of Sight and Non-Blindness
Objection against conceptual identity of sight and its negated negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two coexistent concepts can still be conceptually distinct, using the example of sight and the negation of its negation.

Core Idea
Conceptual dependence differs from existential coexistence; one may depend on the other but not vice versa.

In this passage, a sharp objection is raised against the claim that 'non-blindness' is identical to sight itself. The objection, drawn from glosses on al-Tajrid, shows that conceptual dependence is not symmetric: sight does not require its negation to be conceived, but the negation of its negation does. Therefore, they cannot be the same concept, even if they always occur together. This lesson will walk through the reasoning and clarify a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim being refuted
Start by reading the Arabic and translation. Notice that the objection begins with 'He said: (So it is sight itself) was refuted...' Understand that the target claim is that 'non-blindness' is conceptually identical to sight.
The claim may seem intuitive, but the objection shows it is mistaken.
2
Examine the two premises about conceptual dependence
The objection states: (1) conceiving sight does not require conceiving its negation; (2) conceiving the negation of the negation of sight does require conceiving sight. This asymmetry is key.
Think about why this asymmetry matters: if two concepts are identical, they should have the same conceptual dependencies.
3
Understand the conclusion of non-identity
Because the conceptual dependencies are different, the two cannot be identical 'in concept' (مفهوماً). The objection uses the word 'قطعاً' (definitely) to stress certainty.
Pay attention to the logical force: the conclusion is not just plausible but necessary.
4
Reflect on the additional point about coexistence
The objection adds that even if the two are coexistent (متلازمان), the difference is not merely linguistic (just the negative particle 'سلب'). The difference is conceptual.
This guards against the misconception that coexistence implies conceptual identity or that the difference is only verbal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فهو البصر بعينه رده في حواشي التجريد بأن تعقل البصر لا يتوقف على تعقل انتفائه، وتعقل سلب انتفاء البصر يتوقف عليه قطعاً، فلا يتحدان مفهوماً قطعاً. وإن كانا متلازمين فليس الاختلاف بينهما لمجرد حرف السلب في اللفظ فقط .
He said: (So it is sight itself) was refuted in the glosses on al-Tajrid by saying that the conception of sight does not depend on the conception of its negation, while the conception of the negation of the negation of sight depends on it necessarily, so they are definitely not identical in concept. Even if they are coexistent, the difference between them is not merely due to the negative particle in the wording.

Commentary
This passage presents a specific objection from the glosses on al-Tajrid against the earlier claim that 'non-blindness' (بمعنى سلب انتفاء البصر) is identical to sight itself. The objection argues that the conception of sight does not require the conception of its negation, but the conception of 'negation of the negation of sight' does require it. Therefore, they are not conceptually identical. Moreover, the difference is not merely linguistic (just a negative particle) but conceptual. This shows that even if they are coexistent in existence, their concepts differ. The passage thus guards against conflating conceptual difference with existential coextensiveness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that if two things always occur together (e.g., sight and non-blindness), they must be conceptually identical, and any difference is just verbal.
Correction: The objection shows that conceptual identity requires symmetric dependence; since one concept depends on the other but not vice versa, they are different concepts despite coexistence.

Key Takeaway
Two concepts can be coexistent yet differ conceptually if one depends on the other but not vice versa, as demonstrated by sight and the negation of its negation.

Classification of Non-Blindness: Negation and Affirmation
Analysis of classification of اللاعمى under سلب وإيجاب with objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the general concept of اللاعمى (non-blindness) is classified under سلب وإيجاب (negation and affirmation) rather than العدم والملكة (privation and faculty), despite its apparent privative nature.

Core Idea
Non-blindness is not a simple privation; its opposition to blindness is between capacity and its negation.

This passage delves into a subtle classification within the theory of opposition. While اللاعمى (non-blindness) appears to be a privation opposite to blindness, the author argues that its general concept falls under negation and affirmation, not privation and faculty. The discussion involves objections and clarifications about the role of the subject's capacity and the redefinition of 'existent' in this context. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Notice the dense argumentation with objections and answers.
The passage is continuous; read it as a whole first.
2
Identify the main claim and objections
The author claims the opposition between العمى and اللاعمى is under سلب وإيجاب. Two objections are raised: (1) the general concept opposes directly, and (2) عدم اللازم opposes وجود الملزوم outside the categories.
The first objection is about the general concept; the second is about a different pair.
3
Understand the author's answer and refinement
The answer states that opposition requires a single subject, so the second objection fails. Then the author refines the definition of 'existent' to include pairs like العمى and اللاعمى under سلب وإيجاب.
Note the redefinition: 'existent' here means whose negation is not part of its concept.
4
Clarify the real nature of opposition
The apparent opposition between two privations is actually between capacity (to see) and negation of capacity. This is why it falls under سلب وإيجاب.
The general concept of اللاعمى does not consider the subject's capacity, so it is a negation of the capacity.

He said: (And if the negation of capacity is intended, then the opposition between them is by affirmation and negation) or it was objected that if he intends that the opposition of 'non-blindness' in the sense of negation of capacity with 'blindness' is opposition of negation and affirmation, it is denied. Even if it is granted that the concept of 'non-blindness' is more general than each of 'negation of absence' and 'negation of capacity', and this more general concept is opposed to the concept of 'blindness' in itself, then the opposition between the two privations is established. Secondly, that the absence of the necessary concomitant (عدم اللازم) opposes the existence of the necessitating (وجود الملزوم), and it is not included in 'privation and faculty' nor in 'negation and affirmation', because what is considered in them is that the non-existent of the two be a negation of the existent. And it was answered that the two correlatives are measured relative to a single subject, and there is no doubt that the absence of the necessary concomitant and the existence of the necessitating differ in subject, so there is no opposition between them. It was objected that the discussion is about the existence of the necessitating in the subject and the absence of the necessary concomitant from that subject, like the existence of motion in the body with the absence of the heat that is necessary for it. And the author deviated from the well-known to his statement: 'Either one of them is not a negation of the other, or it is', as a hint that what is meant by 'existent' here is that whose negation is not part of its concept. Thus, such as 'blindness' and 'non-blindness' enter into the second division, i.e., that one of the two correlatives is a negation of the other, and it is necessary that it be of the type of negation and affirmation, because the concept of 'non-blindness' in its general aspect does not consider the capacity of the subject. As for the absence of the necessary concomitant with the existence of the necessitating, it entered. He said: (Differing in subject) because one is measured relative to the necessary concomitant and the other to the necessitating. So the objective of the objector is achieved, since his aim is to establish an opposition between the two privations. And if he intends that the opposition of negation of capacity is opposition of negation and affirmation, that is denied, but there is no talk about it; the talk is about the opposition of capacity with... much benefit. He said: (With the absence of the heat that is necessary for it) this is by way of example, or the intended meaning is the elemental body, so the discussion about the necessity by the existence of motion in the celestial sphere with the absence of heat in it is not for it. He said: (As for that what is meant by the existent, etc.) It was said that making such as 'blindness' and 'sight' then from 'privation and faculty' is artificial, since negation is not part of its concept but is itself, so it would necessitate them being from the two opposites. He said: (And as for the absence of the necessary concomitant) is an objection to the author and his statement: 'with their explicit statement' is from the completion of the entry, and it does not admit of approval at all, as thought, because the addition is considered, so negation is a part of the total definitely, as I verified.

Commentary
This lesson covers the detailed discussion of the general concept of 'non-blindness' (اللاعمى). The passage begins by considering an objection: if 'non-blindness' is taken as 'negation of capacity', its opposition to 'blindness' might be considered negation and affirmation, but the objector argues that the concept of 'non-blindness' is more general than both specific interpretations, and this general concept directly opposes 'blindness', thus establishing an opposition between two privations. Additionally, the objector argues that the absence of a necessary concomitant (عدم اللازم) opposes the existence of the necessitating (وجود الملزوم), and this is neither 'privation and faculty' nor 'negation and affirmation'. The answer explains that correlatives must be measured to a single subject, and here the subjects differ, so there is no opposition. The objector then gives an example: motion in a body with absence of its necessary heat. The author responds by redefining 'existent' here as that whose negation is not part of its concept. Thus, pairs like 'blindness' and 'non-blindness' fall into the second division (one is negation of the other) and are of the type 'negation and affirmation', because the general concept of 'non-blindness' does not consider the subject's capacity. The passage then clarifies that the apparent opposition between two privations is actually between capacity and negation of capacity. Later, it addresses an objection that calling 'blindness' and 'sight' as 'privation and faculty' is artificial, since 'blindness' is an added privation with an existential component, so it is not problematic. Finally, it touches on the objection regarding 'absence of the necessary concomitant' and affirms that negation is part of the total when addition is considered.
